Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
Refutation of Mu'tazila Claim of Consensus on Fisq
Refutation of Mu'tazila argument and presentation of correct methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's claim of consensus on calling a major sinner a fasiq is invalid and learn the correct methodology for handling scholarly disagreements.

Core Idea
True consensus cannot be claimed by introducing a new opinion that contradicts earlier agreement.

The Mu'tazila argued that there is consensus that a major sinner is called a fasiq, and that names like mu'min and kafir are disputed, so we should stick with the agreed term. This passage refutes that claim by showing that their own position is a novel one that violates prior consensus. It also outlines the proper approach when encountering differing views: examine them against evidence, and if unable to decide, pause and consult a qualified scholar.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
The Mu'tazila claim that consensus exists on calling the grave sinner a fasiq while other labels are disputed. They argue we should take the agreed term and leave the disputed ones.
This is the position being refuted.
2
Analyze the refutation
The author points out that before the Mu'tazila, the community had three positions and agreed that any other position is false. By introducing a fourth, the Mu'tazila themselves contradict consensus.
The key point: the Mu'tazila's 'consensus' is based on ignoring their own innovation.
3
Learn the correct methodology
When differences arise, one must investigate the views using evidence to distinguish truth from falsehood. If unable to decide, one should pause (tawaqquf) and consult a knowledgeable scholar.
This is the alternative to the Mu'tazila's flawed reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ما تزعم المعتزلة أن إطلاق اسم الفسق مجمع عليه وإطلاق ما وراء ذلك من اسم المؤمن والكافر والمنافق مختلف فيه فأخذنا بالمجمع عليه وتركنا المختلف فيه. كلام باطل، إذ هو أخذ بالمجمع عليه وأعرض عن المخالفة، وتحقيقه ما هي المخالفة؟ فإن الأمة قبل ظهور نحلتهم إذا كانوا على ثلاثة أقوال كانوا مجتمعين على أن ما وراءها قول باطل، فهم إذا أحدثوا قولاً رابعا فقد خالفوا الإجماع، وكذا أجمعوا أن لا منزلة بين الإيمان والكفر، فمن أثبت المنزلة فقد خالف الأمة، والأخذ بالإجماع بما هو مخالف للإجماع من وجهين جهل فاحش. ثم الأمة إذا اختلفت في شيء على أقاويل لم يجز الخروج عن أقاويلهم لما مر أن ذلك منهم إجماع أن ما عدا هذه الأقاويل باطل، فبعد ذلك فالواجب البحث عن الأقاويل وعرضها على الدلائل ليتبين الصحيح من الفاسد والحق من الباطل، وعند العجز عن التمييز بين الحق منها والباطل يجب التوقف والرجوع إلى من أكرم بالعلم والخضوع له والجثو بين يديه ليبين له ذلك، فأما جعل التوقف الذي هو مقتضى تعارض الأدلة ونتيجة العجز عن ترجيح بعض الأقاويل على بعض موجبات الحيرة - مذهبًا يتمسك به وعقيدة يدان بها ويناضل عنها ويناظر مخالفوها فيحيد عما توجبه العقول وتقتضيه الأصول.
Then what the Mu'tazila claim that the application of the name 'fasiq' is agreed upon (mujma' 'alayhi) and the application beyond that of the names 'mu'min', 'kafir', and 'munafiq' is disputed, so we take the agreed upon and leave the disputed. This is false speech, for it is taking the agreed upon while turning away from opposition. And its verification is: what is the opposition? Indeed the community (umma) before the emergence of their sect, when they were upon three statements, were agreed that what is beyond them is a false statement. So when they invented a fourth statement, they opposed the consensus. Likewise, they agreed that there is no intermediate station (manzila) between faith and disbelief. So whoever affirms the intermediate station has opposed the community. And taking the consensus by what opposes the consensus in two respects is gross ignorance. Then when the community differs on something according to statements, it is not permissible to go outside their statements, because it has passed that from them is a consensus that other than these statements is false. After that, the obligation is to investigate the statements and present them to the evidences to distinguish the sound from the corrupt and the truth from falsehood. And when unable to distinguish between the truth and falsehood among them, it is necessary to pause (tawaqquf) and return to one who is honored with knowledge and submission to him and kneeling before him so that he may explain that. As for making pause, which is the requirement of conflicting evidences and the result of inability to prefer some statements over other causes of confusion, into a doctrine (madhhab) to be adhered to and a creed to be practiced and for which one fights and debates its opponents, then it deviates from what the intellects require and the principles necessitate.
Source pages p. 1048

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila claim that there is consensus (ijma') on calling a major sinner a fasiq (sinner) while the names mu'min, kafir, and munafiq are disputed, so they argue one should take the agreed name (fasiq) and leave the disputed ones. The author calls this false because it takes the consensus while ignoring the opposition. He explains that before the Mu'tazila emerged, the community (umma) was on three positions (likely those of Kharijites, Murji'ah, and others), and they were agreed that any position beyond those three is false. The Mu'tazila introduced a fourth position (the intermediate station between faith and disbelief), thus violating that consensus. He further states that the community agreed there is no intermediate station between iman and kufr. Therefore, the Mu'tazila's claim of consensus is self-contradictory. The author then discusses proper methodology: when differences arise, one must examine the views against evidence to distinguish truth from falsehood. If one is unable to decide, one must pause (tawaqquf) and consult a qualified scholar. However, making tawaqquf itself into a doctrine to be fought for is wrong, as it deviates from what reason and principles require.

Key Terms
الفسق (fisq)
الإجماع (ijma')
المنزلة (manzila)
التوقف (tawaqquf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That consensus can be selectively applied to support one's position while ignoring that the position itself violates an earlier consensus.
Correction: True consensus requires that the opinion itself does not contradict prior agreement. The Mu'tazila's claim is invalid because their 'fasiq' label presupposes an intermediate station, which the community had already agreed does not exist.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the Mu'tazila's claim of consensus on the name 'fasiq'?
A) Because the Mu'tazila fabricated the consensus.
B) Because the community before them was agreed that there are only three positions, and their fourth position opposes that original consensus.
C) Because the name 'fasiq' is not found in the Quran.
D) Because the Mu'tazila do not accept consensus as a proof.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's claim of consensus is invalid because their position itself contradicts prior communal agreement; the correct approach is to investigate differing views against evidence and pause when uncertain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a disagreement among scholars, do you tend to lazily follow the view that feels most comfortable or popular, rather than investigating the evidence with humility? In what specific area of your deen are you most tempted to take the path of least resistance—even if it may contradict the way of the Salaf?
?
The Mu'tazila prided themselves on 'following consensus' while unknowingly opposing it. Is there a belief or practice in your life that you justify by saying 'everyone does it' or 'this is the accepted view,' while your conscience whispers that it might not align with the Quran and Sunnah? Will you honestly examine that area today?
?
The author teaches that when we are unable to discern truth, we must pause and submit to a knowledgeable teacher with humility—like kneeling before him. Have you ever clung to a religious opinion out of stubbornness or pride, refusing to consult someone more learned because you didn't want to be corrected? Can you commit to seeking guidance with the posture of a student, not a debater?

Quranic Evidence That Sin Does Not Negate Faith
Quranic evidence that the name of faith remains despite sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran preserves the title of 'believer' for sinners, proving that sin does not remove faith.

Core Idea
Sinners are still called believers in the Quran.

The Maturidi position holds that sin does not nullify faith. This lesson presents three Quranic verses where Allah addresses individuals as 'believers' despite their sinful actions. These verses serve as direct evidence that the name of faith remains even when one commits sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation. Notice the three verses cited and the overall argument.
The passage begins by stating this is evidence for Ahl al-Haqq's position.
2
Identify the three verses
List the three Quranic verses mentioned. For each, note what sin is present and how the address 'believers' is used.
The verses are from Surah al-Nisa', al-Hujurat, and al-Baqarah.
3
Analyze the implication of each verse
For each verse, consider: if sin removed faith, would Allah still call these people 'believers'? The fact that He does shows sin does not negate faith.
Pay special attention to the verse about fighting groups, where one is rebellious (baghiya).
4
Reflect on the argumentative structure
Notice that the author uses the preservation of the name 'iman' as proof. Also note that the last verse contains 'indication from three aspects' – think about what those might be.
The passage cuts off, but the main argument is clear: the Quranic usage itself supports the Maturidi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على صحة ما ذهب إليه أهل الحق أن الله تعالى أبقى اسم الإيمان مع وجود ما عليه الوعيد بقوله تعالى: {يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا لَا تَقْرَبُوا الصِّلَوْةَ وَأَنتُمْ سُكَرَى} وقوله تعالى: {وَإِن طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا فَأَصْلِحُوا بَيْنَهُمَا} أبقى لهما اسم الإيمان مع أن أحديهما باغية، وقوله تعالى: {يَاأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ} وفي الآية دلالة من ثلاثة أوجه:
Then the evidence for the correctness of what Ahl al-Haqq went to is that Allah Almighty preserved the name of faith (iman) with the existence of what is warned against, by His statement: 'O you who have believed, do not approach prayer while you are drunk' (al-Nisa' 4:43), and His statement: 'And if two groups among the believers fight, then make peace between them' (al-Hujurat 49:9) – He preserved for them the name of faith although one of them is aggressive, and His statement: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is legal retribution' (al-Baqarah 2:178). And in the verse there is indication from three aspects:
Source pages p. 1049

Commentary
This passage presents a Quranic argument for the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., the Maturidi/Ash'ari position) that sin does not remove faith. Allah addresses believers as 'O you who have believed' while prohibiting them from approaching prayer while drunk (Surah al-Nisa', 4:43). This shows that even with the sin of drinking, the address 'believers' remains. Similarly, in Surah al-Hujurat (49:9), Allah calls two fighting groups 'believers' even though one group is rebellious (baghiyah). Thus, the name of faith is preserved despite the sin of fighting and rebellion. In Surah al-Baqarah (2:178), Allah addresses 'O you who have believed' while prescribing retribution (qisas), which implies that believers may commit crimes deserving punishment. The passage notes that in this verse there is indication from three aspects, but the text cuts off before listing them. The overall point is that these verses demonstrate that sin does not negate the status of being a believer.

Key Terms
أهل الحق (Ahl al-Haqq)
الوعيد (wa'id)
المؤمنين (mu'minun)
الباغية (baghiya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quranic addresses to 'believers' only apply to sinless individuals.
Correction: These verses show that Allah addresses sinners as 'believers', proving sin does not negate the name of faith.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does the author use to show that fighting believers are still called 'believers'?
A) 'O you who have believed, do not approach prayer while you are drunk.'
B) 'And if two groups among the believers fight, then make peace between them.'
C) 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is legal retribution.'
D) 'O you who have believed, fear Allah as He should be feared.'

Key Takeaway
Allah's preservation of the name 'believers' for sinners in the Quran proves that sin does not remove faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you feel that your identity as a believer is shaken? How does knowing that Allah still calls you 'believer' in the Quran change how you view yourself after sin?
?
Think of a person you know who is openly committing a major sin. Do you subconsciously think of them as less of a believer? How can the verse about the two fighting groups (both called believers) challenge that judgment?
?
If you were in a position of conflict with another Muslim, would you still see them as a believer? How does this lesson compel you to preserve the bond of faith even in disagreement?

Proof from the Retaliation Verse: Three Aspects
Threefold proof from retaliation verse that sinners remain believers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's retaliation verse (2:178) proves that a believer does not lose the name of faith even after committing a major sin like murder, and the three aspects used to establish this.

Core Idea
The Quran itself calls sinners 'believers' — proving faith survives major sins.

The People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) argued that committing a major sin does not remove one from the fold of faith. They supported this with Quranic evidence, especially the retaliation verse. In this lesson, we examine the three aspects extracted from that verse, showing how Allah preserves the name of faith, brotherhood, and mercy for the sinner.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the three Quranic verses cited and the three aspects of the retaliation verse.
2
Identify the supporting verses
Before the retaliation verse, the author cites two other verses (4:43 and 49:9) that also address sinners as believers. Observe how they establish the principle.
3
Examine the three aspects
Focus on the retaliation verse itself. The author lists three distinct ways it preserves faith, brotherhood, and mercy for the murderer. Identify each one.
The three aspects are: 1) name of faith, 2) brotherhood, 3) entitlement to mercy.
4
Understand the significance
Consider why these three aspects collectively show that major sin does not negate faith. The deduction is attributed to Abdullah ibn Mas'ud.
This proof is foundational for the Maturidi position on faith and works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على صحة ما ذهب إليه أهل الحق أن الله تعالى أبقى اسم الإيمان مع وجود ما عليه الوعيد بقوله تعالى: ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَقْرَبُوا الصَّلَاةَ وَأَنْتُمْ سُكَارَى ﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَإِنْ طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا فَأَصْلِحُوا بَيْنَهُمَا ﴾ أبقى لهما اسم الإيمان مع أن أحديهما باغية، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ ﴾ وفي الآية دلالة من ثلاثة أوجه: أحدها: أنه أبقى اسم الإيمان مع وجوب القصاص الذي هو حكم قتل العمد الخالي عن الشبه كلها، ولا شك في كونه كبيرة. والثاني: أنه أبقى اسم الأخوة الثابتة بالإيمان بقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّمَا الْمُؤْمِنُونَ إِخْوَةٌ ﴾ بين القاتل وأولياء المقتول بقوله: ﴿ فَمَنْ عُفِيَ لَهُ مِنْ أَخِيهِ شَيْءٌ فَاتِّبَاعٌ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ ﴾ الآية. والثالث: أنه تعالى ما أخرج مرتكب هذه الكبيرة عن استئهال التخفيف والمرحمة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ ذَلِكَ تَخْفِيفٌ مِنْ رَبِّكُمْ وَرَحْمَةٌ ﴾ وحكى الاستدلال من هذه الأوجه الثلاثة على نحو ما بينا عن عبد الله بن مسعود – رضي الله عنهما -
Then the proof for the correctness of what the People of Truth went to is that Allah, exalted, preserved the name of faith with the existence of what entails threat in His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not approach prayer while you are drunk.' And His saying: 'And if two factions among the believers fight, then make peace between them.' He preserved for them the name of faith while one of them is transgressing. And His saying: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is retaliation.' And in the verse there is indication from three aspects: One: He preserved the name of faith with the obligation of retaliation, which is the judgment of intentional killing devoid of all doubts, and there is no doubt that it is a major sin. The second: He preserved the name of brotherhood established by faith in His saying: 'The believers are but brothers' between the killer and the heirs of the slain in His saying: 'Then whoever has been pardoned from his brother something, then follow-up according to custom.' The third: He, exalted, did not remove the committer of this major sin from being deserving of alleviation and mercy in His saying: 'That is an alleviation from your Lord and a mercy.' And he narrated the inference from these three aspects in the manner we mentioned from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud – may Allah be pleased with them.
Source pages p. 1049

Commentary
This passage presents a scriptural proof from the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) that a believer does not lose the name of faith by committing a major sin. The author begins by citing three Quranic verses. First, 4:43 addresses believers as 'O you who have believed' even as it prohibits them from praying while drunk — a sin. Second, 49:9 calls fighting factions 'believers' despite their transgression, preserving the name of faith. Third, 2:178 (the retaliation verse) addresses believers and prescribes capital punishment for intentional murder, a major sin. The author then extracts three specific points from this last verse: 1) Allah preserved the name 'believer' for someone obligated to undergo retaliation — clearly a major sin. 2) He preserved the bond of brotherhood from faith between the killer and the victim's family, as shown by the phrase 'from his brother.' 3) He did not remove the murderer from being entitled to alleviation and mercy (by allowing pardon and compensation), which is expressed in the same verse: 'That is an alleviation from your Lord and a mercy.' The author notes that this threefold deduction is transmitted from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الكبيرة
القصاص
الأخوة
التخفيف
المرحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Addressing a sinner as 'believer' in the Quran is merely metaphorical or honorific, not indicative of actual faith.
Correction: The author shows that the Quran preserves real faith, brotherhood, and mercy for the sinner, indicating genuine faith remains.

Checkpoint
How many aspects of the retaliation verse are used to prove that the sinner remains a believer?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The retaliation verse proves that even a murderer retains the name of faith, brotherhood, and entitlement to mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah calls a murderer a 'believer' and preserves brotherhood with the victim's family, how does this change the way you view a fellow Muslim who has committed a serious sin? Are you quicker to cut ties than Allah is?
?
The verse shows that mercy is available even for the murderer who has not yet repented. Where in your life have you denied yourself or others that same mercy because you felt the sin was too great?
?
How does knowing that a major sin does not remove the name of faith affect your sense of responsibility towards a brother or sister who is struggling with sin? What concrete step can you take this week to show them brotherhood?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
5 min
Quranic Evidence: Believers Despite Sins
The author cites more Quranic verses preserving faith for sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran preserves the name of 'believer' for sinners through multiple verses, and why this is evidence in kalam for the Maturidi position on faith and action.

Core Idea
Even grave sins do not remove the name of faith.

This passage continues the Maturidi proof from the Quran. The author cites verses where Allah addresses those who commit sins—like failing to emigrate, betraying trusts, or taking enemies as allies—yet still calls them 'believers.' This shows that faith remains even with major sins, challenging those who claim sins expel one from faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the sequence of verses cited.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the argument is clear.
2
Identify each verse and its sin
List each verse cited: 8:72 (not emigrating), 4:97 (threat for failing to emigrate), 60:1 (taking enemies as allies), 8:27 (betraying trusts), 24:31 (repentance commanded). Note the sin mentioned.
3
Understand the argument
Despite the sins, Allah still addresses them as 'believers' (e.g., 'O you who have believed'). This proves that sin does not negate faith.
The author also mentions the ummah's practice of praying over such individuals.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
This supports the Maturidi view that faith is distinct from action; major sins do not remove one from the fold of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَلَمْ يُهَاجِرُوا ﴾ أبقى لمن لم يهاجر اسم الإيمان مع عظم الوعيد في ترك الهجرة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ تَوَفَّاهُمُ الْمَلَائِكَةُ ظَالِمِي أَنْفُسِهِمْ ﴾ مع أنه قال في آخر الآية: ﴿ مَا لَكُمْ مِنْ وَلَايَتِهِمْ مِنْ شَيْءٍ ﴾ ثم هذا جعلهم مؤمنين وأوجب على المؤمنين نصرهم عند استنصارهم وقوله: ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَتَّخِذُوا عَدُوِّي وَعَدُوَّكُمْ أَوْلِيَاءَ ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَخُونُوا اللَّهَ وَالرَّسُولَ وَتَخُونُوا أَمَانَاتِكُمْ ﴾ وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَتُوبُوا إِلَى اللَّهِ جَمِيعًا أَيُّهَ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ لَعَلَّكُمْ تُفْلِحُونَ ﴾ وفي الباب آيات كثيرة، وكذا الأمة بأسرهم توارثوا الصلاة على
And His saying: 'And those who believed and did not emigrate' — He preserved for those who did not emigrate the name of faith despite the great threat in abandoning emigration in His saying: 'Indeed, those whom the angels take in death while wronging themselves' along with His saying at the end of the verse: 'For you there is no guardianship of them at all.' Then this made them believers and obligated the believers to support them when they seek help. And His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not take My enemy and your enemy as allies.' And His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not betray Allah and the Messenger and betray your trusts.' And His saying, exalted: 'And repent to Allah, all of you, O believers, that you may succeed.' And concerning this topic there are many verses. And likewise, the entire ummah inherited prayer upon...
Source pages p. 1050

Commentary
This passage continues the proof from Ahl al-Haqq by citing additional Quranic verses. The first is 8:72, which distinguishes between believers who emigrated and those who did not. Despite the severe warning in 4:97 about those who fail to emigrate, the verse still calls them believers. It even obligates other believers to support them militarily. Then follow several more verses that address sinners as believers: 60:1 prohibits taking enemies as allies while addressing 'O you who have believed'; 8:27 prohibits betrayal while addressing 'O you who have believed'; 24:31 commands repentance while addressing 'O believers.' The author remarks that many verses exist on this theme, and that the entire ummah has inherited the practice of praying (presumably funeral prayer) over such individuals, further confirming that they are considered believers.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الهجرة
الوعيد
النصر
التوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that failing to perform an obligation like emigration nullifies one's faith and renders one a disbeliever.
Correction: These verses show that Allah still addresses such individuals as 'believers' and even commands other believers to support them, proving that faith remains despite the sin.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Allah call those who did not emigrate despite the threat?
A) Disbelievers
B) Hypocrites
C) Believers
D) Sinners only

Key Takeaway
The Quran consistently preserves the title 'believer' for those who commit sins, confirming that faith is not nullified by sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely committed sins that you consider grave. Does this lesson give you a false sense of security because you are still called a believer? Or does it drive you to repentance as the verse commands? What is your immediate response to the call 'Repent to Allah, O believers'? Will you repent now, or will you delay?
?
Think of a fellow Muslim who has fallen into a major sin you know of. Do you treat them as a believer deserving support, or do you distance yourself from them? The lesson shows they are still believers and you are obligated to support them. How will you change your attitude toward them this week?
?
When you read the verse 'O you who have believed, do not betray Allah and the Messenger,' do you examine your own betrayals? What specific betrayal of Allah or His Messenger are you tolerating in your life right now? Write down one step you will take today to stop that betrayal and be a true believer.

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
4 min
The First Proof: Faith and Qisas
First Quranic proof: faith name remains with qisas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the obligation of qisas for murder demonstrates that the name of faith remains despite committing a major sin.

Core Idea
Qisas proves faith survives major sins.

Nasafi begins his argument by pointing to a clear Quranic ruling: the obligation of retaliation (qisas) for intentional murder. Even though murder is unquestionably a major sin, Allah still calls the killer a believer in the context of qisas laws. This shows that the name of faith remains even when a major sin is committed.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get its rhythm. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
2
Identify the key components
Notice the three elements: the name of faith is preserved, qisas is obligatory for intentional murder, and murder is a major sin.
The argument relies on the combination of these three facts.
3
Understand the logic
Ask yourself: If major sins removed faith entirely, how could the Quran still call the killer a believer in the qisas verse? The preservation of the name 'faith' proves that major sin does not nullify faith.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
This is the first of three proofs that refute the Kharijite and Mutazilite claim that major sins destroy faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أنه أبقى اسم الإيمان مع وجوب القصاص الذي هو حكم قتل العمد الخالي عن الشبه كلها، ولا شك في كونه كبيرة. والثاني: أنه أبقى اسم الأخوة الثابتة بالإيمان بقوله تعالى: إِنَّمَا الْمُؤْمِنُونَ إِخْوَةٌ بين القاتل وأولياء المقتول بقوله: فَمَنْ عُفِى لَهُ مِنْ أَخِيهِ شَيْءٌ فَانْبَاعُ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ الآية. والثالث : أنه تعالى ما أخرج مرتكب هذه الكبيرة عن استئهال التخفيف والمرحمة بقوله تعالى: ذَلِكَ تَخْفِيفٌ مِن رَّبِّكُمْ وَرَحْمَةٌ وحكى الاستدلال من هذه الأوجه الثلاثة على نحو ما بينا عن عبد الله بن مسعود – رضي الله عنهما - وقوله تعالى: وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَلَمْ يُهَاجِرُوا أبقى لمن لم يهاجر اسم الإيمان مع عظم الوعيد في ترك الهجرة بقوله تعالى: إِنَّ الَّذِينَ تَوَفَّهُمُ الْمَلَتَيكَةُ ظَالِمِي أَنفُسِهِمْ مع أنه قال في آخر الآية: مَا لَكُم مِّن وَلَيَتِهِم مِّن شَيْءٍ ثم هذا جعلهم مؤمنين وأوجب على المؤمنين نصرهم عند استنصارهم وقوله: يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا لَا تَتَّخِذُوا عَدُوّى وَعَدُوكُمْ أَقلياة وقوله : تَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا غَفُوتُوا اللَّهَ أَوْلِيَاءَ وَالرَّسُولَ وَتَخُونُوا أَمَنَتِكُمْ وقوله تعالى: وَتُوبُوا إِلَى اللَّهِ جَمِيعًا أَيُّهَ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ لَعَلَّكُمْ تفلحون وفي الباب آيات كثيرة، وكذا الأمة بأسرهم توارثوا الصلاة على من مات من أهل القبلة والاستغفار لهم والترحم عليهم من غير التفحص عن حالة أنه ارتكب كبيرة أو لم يرتكب.
First: He (Allah) preserved the name of faith along with the obligation of retaliation (qisas) which is the ruling for intentional murder devoid of all ambiguities, and there is no doubt that it is a major sin. Second: He preserved the name of brotherhood established by faith in His saying: 'The believers are but brothers' between the killer and the heirs of the slain in His saying: 'So whoever is granted something from his brother, let it be followed with kindness' the verse. Third: He, exalted, did not expel the perpetrator of this major sin from being deserving of lightening and mercy in His saying: 'That is a lightening from your Lord and mercy.' And he narrated the inference from these three aspects as we explained from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud – may Allah be pleased with them both – and His saying: 'And those who believed but did not emigrate' He preserved the name of faith for those who did not emigrate despite the severity of the threat for abandoning emigration in His saying: 'Indeed those whom the angels take in death while they are wronging themselves' while He said at the end of the verse: 'You have nothing of their guardianship' then this made them believers and obliged the believers to help them when they sought help, and His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not take My enemy and your enemy as allies' and His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not betray Allah and the Messenger and betray your trusts' and His saying: 'And turn to Allah in repentance, all of you, O believers, that you might succeed.' And in this regard there are many verses, and likewise the entire Ummah have transmitted to each other the prayer upon whoever dies from the people of the qibla, and asking forgiveness for them, and having mercy on them, without examining his state whether he committed a major sin or not.
Source pages p. 1050

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments from the Quran to prove that the name of faith remains with a believer who commits a major sin. The first argument: Allah preserved the name of faith for the one who commits intentional murder while also imposing the obligation of retaliation (qisas). Since murder is undoubtedly a major sin, yet the Quran still calls such a person a believer (as implied by the laws of qisas that apply between believers), this shows that the major sin does not negate faith. The second argument: Allah preserves the brotherhood of faith between the killer and the heirs of the slain, as seen in the verse of qisas where He says 'from his brother.' This indicates that despite the killing, the killer remains a brother in faith. The third argument: Allah does not remove the perpetrator of this major sin from being eligible for alleviation and mercy, as He says 'That is a lightening from your Lord and mercy.' Then the author mentions that this reasoning is similar to what Abdullah ibn Mas'ud narrated. Additional evidence is drawn from verses about those who did not emigrate: despite the severe threat for not emigrating, Allah still calls them 'believers' and orders other believers to support them. Further verses are cited where Allah addresses believers with commands and prohibitions, showing that the address of faith remains effective. Finally, the author notes the universal practice of the Muslim community: they pray for all who die facing the qibla, seek forgiveness for them, and show mercy to them, without investigating whether they committed major sins. This consensus supports that such people are considered believers despite their sins.

Key Terms
إيمان (faith)
كبيرة (major sin)
قصاص (retaliation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some groups (Kharijites, Mutazilites) claim that committing a major sin nullifies faith and makes one a disbeliever or in a state between faith and disbelief.
Correction: This passage shows that the Quran itself contradicts this claim by preserving the name of faith for the killer in the qisas verse, proving that major sin does not remove faith.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, which Quranic ruling preserves the name of faith despite a major sin?
A) The obligation of fasting
B) The obligation of qisas (retaliation)
C) The prohibition of stealing
D) The command to pray

Key Takeaway
The preservation of the name 'faith' alongside qisas shows that major sins do not strip a person of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely committed sins you consider major. Do you truly believe your faith remains intact? How does this proof challenge any secret doubt you might have about your own standing with Allah? Will you let this certainty change how you view yourself and your relationship with Him?
?
Think of a fellow Muslim who has fallen into a major sin. Do you ever question their faith? Based on this lesson, what must you affirm about them? What does that require of you in how you treat them?
?
This proof shows that Allah preserved the name of faith even for a murderer. How does that reality confront your tendency to judge others harshly? What will you do today to align your heart with this divine mercy?

The Second Proof: Brotherhood Preserved
Second proof: brotherhood in faith between killer and heirs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse referring to the killer as 'brother' of the victim's heirs proves that faith remains despite major sin.

Core Idea
Brotherhood in faith survives even murder.

The second proof draws on the verse in Surah al-Hujurat that declares all believers are brothers. In the context of qisas, Allah says 'so whoever is granted something from his brother' — referring to the killer as a brother of the victim's heirs. This brotherhood is based on faith, so preserving it means faith remains despite the killing.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the key phrase 'from his brother' referring to the killer.
2
Identify the two key verses
One verse establishes universal brotherhood among believers. The other uses 'brother' for the killer in the qisas context.
3
Analyze the implication
If the killer were not a believer, he could not be called a brother. Since brotherhood is based on faith, calling him brother proves his faith remains intact.
4
Reflect on the refutation
This directly refutes the claim that major sins remove one from the fold of faith.

The Third Proof: Eligibility for Mercy
Third proof: qisas as lightening and mercy shows faith remains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse declaring qisas as a lightening and mercy from Allah proves that the major sinner is still eligible for divine mercy.

Core Idea
Allah's mercy still reaches the major sinner.

The third proof comes from the same qisas verse where Allah says 'That is a lightening from your Lord and mercy.' This shows that the perpetrator of the major sin is still within the scope of divine alleviation and mercy, which would not be the case if he were a disbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the key phrase 'lightening from your Lord and mercy'.
2
Understand the context
The 'lightening' refers to the permission of qisas (rather than immediate capital punishment), showing Allah's mercy towards the killer.
3
Draw the conclusion
Because the killer is still considered a believer, he is eligible for Allah's mercy. If he had left faith, he would not be addressed with mercy.
4
Connect to the overall argument
This third proof completes the set: faith name, brotherhood, and mercy all remain despite major sin.

?

?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
5 min
Additional Evidence: Emigration and Consensus
Additional Quranic verses and consensus support faith for sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic verses about those who did not emigrate and the consensus of the Ummah further confirm that faith remains despite major sins.

Core Idea
Scripture and consensus affirm faith for sinners.

After presenting the three core proofs, Nasafi adds supporting evidence: verses about believers who did not emigrate still being called believers despite a severe threat, and the unanimous practice of the Ummah praying for all who die facing the qibla regardless of their sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic from 'و حكى الاستدلال...' to the end. Note the multiple verses cited.
2
Analyze the emigration verses
Notice that Allah calls those who did not emigrate 'believers' and commands support for them, despite their disobedience.
3
Examine the consensus argument
The Ummah has always prayed for and sought forgiveness for all who die as Muslims without investigating their sins. This consensus shows that such people are considered believers.
4
Synthesize the overall lesson
This additional evidence reinforces the three proofs, building a strong case against the view that major sins nullify faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أنه أبقى اسم الإيمان مع وجوب القصاص الذي هو حكم قتل العمد الخالي عن الشبه كلها، ولا شك في كونه كبيرة. والثاني: أنه أبقى اسم الأخوة الثابتة بالإيمان بقوله تعالى: إِنَّمَا الْمُؤْمِنُونَ إِخْوَةٌ بين القاتل وأولياء المقتول بقوله: فَمَنْ عُفِى لَهُ مِنْ أَخِيهِ شَيْءٌ فَانْبَاعُ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ الآية. والثالث : أنه تعالى ما أخرج مرتكب هذه الكبيرة عن استئهال التخفيف والمرحمة بقوله تعالى: ذَلِكَ تَخْفِيفٌ مِن رَّبِّكُمْ وَرَحْمَةٌ وحكى الاستدلال من هذه الأوجه الثلاثة على نحو ما بينا عن عبد الله بن مسعود – رضي الله عنهما - وقوله تعالى: وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَلَمْ يُهَاجِرُوا أبقى لمن لم يهاجر اسم الإيمان مع عظم الوعيد في ترك الهجرة بقوله تعالى: إِنَّ الَّذِينَ تَوَفَّهُمُ الْمَلَتَيكَةُ ظَالِمِي أَنفُسِهِمْ مع أنه قال في آخر الآية: مَا لَكُم مِّن وَلَيَتِهِم مِّن شَيْءٍ ثم هذا جعلهم مؤمنين وأوجب على المؤمنين نصرهم عند استنصارهم وقوله: يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا لَا تَتَّخِذُوا عَدُوّى وَعَدُوكُمْ أَقلياة وقوله : تَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا غَفُوتُوا اللَّهَ أَوْلِيَاءَ وَالرَّسُولَ وَتَخُونُوا أَمَنَتِكُمْ وقوله تعالى: وَتُوبُوا إِلَى اللَّهِ جَمِيعًا أَيُّهَ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ لَعَلَّكُمْ تفلحون وفي الباب آيات كثيرة، وكذا الأمة بأسرهم توارثوا الصلاة على من مات من أهل القبلة والاستغفار لهم والترحم عليهم من غير التفحص عن حالة أنه ارتكب كبيرة أو لم يرتكب.
First: He (Allah) preserved the name of faith along with the obligation of retaliation (qisas) which is the ruling for intentional murder devoid of all ambiguities, and there is no doubt that it is a major sin. Second: He preserved the name of brotherhood established by faith in His saying: 'The believers are but brothers' between the killer and the heirs of the slain in His saying: 'So whoever is granted something from his brother, let it be followed with kindness' the verse. Third: He, exalted, did not expel the perpetrator of this major sin from being deserving of lightening and mercy in His saying: 'That is a lightening from your Lord and mercy.' And he narrated the inference from these three aspects as we explained from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud – may Allah be pleased with them both – and His saying: 'And those who believed but did not emigrate' He preserved the name of faith for those who did not emigrate despite the severity of the threat for abandoning emigration in His saying: 'Indeed those whom the angels take in death while they are wronging themselves' while He said at the end of the verse: 'You have nothing of their guardianship' then this made them believers and obliged the believers to help them when they sought help, and His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not take My enemy and your enemy as allies' and His saying: 'O you who have believed, do not betray Allah and the Messenger and betray your trusts' and His saying: 'And turn to Allah in repentance, all of you, O believers, that you might succeed.' And in this regard there are many verses, and likewise the entire Ummah have transmitted to each other the prayer upon whoever dies from the people of the qibla, and asking forgiveness for them, and having mercy on them, without examining his state whether he committed a major sin or not.
Source pages p. 1050

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments from the Quran to prove that the name of faith remains with a believer who commits a major sin. The first argument: Allah preserved the name of faith for the one who commits intentional murder while also imposing the obligation of retaliation (qisas). Since murder is undoubtedly a major sin, yet the Quran still calls such a person a believer (as implied by the laws of qisas that apply between believers), this shows that the major sin does not negate faith. The second argument: Allah preserves the brotherhood of faith between the killer and the heirs of the slain, as seen in the verse of qisas where He says 'from his brother.' This indicates that despite the killing, the killer remains a brother in faith. The third argument: Allah does not remove the perpetrator of this major sin from being eligible for alleviation and mercy, as He says 'That is a lightening from your Lord and mercy.' Then the author mentions that this reasoning is similar to what Abdullah ibn Mas'ud narrated. Additional evidence is drawn from verses about those who did not emigrate: despite the severe threat for not emigrating, Allah still calls them 'believers' and orders other believers to support them. Further verses are cited where Allah addresses believers with commands and prohibitions, showing that the address of faith remains effective. Finally, the author notes the universal practice of the Muslim community: they pray for all who die facing the qibla, seek forgiveness for them, and show mercy to them, without investigating whether they committed major sins. This consensus supports that such people are considered believers despite their sins.

Key Terms
هجرة (emigration)
أهل القبلة (people of the qibla)
إجماع (consensus)

D) They only pray if they were known to be sinless

Key Takeaway
The Quran and the consensus of the Ummah confirm that faith remains even when a believer commits major sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you commit to reminding yourself, even after a major sin, that Allah still calls you a believer, and that this should drive you to repentance, not despair?

How can you train your heart to emulate the mercy of the Ummah and pray for every Muslim, trusting in Allah's forgiveness? What specific step will you take this week to change that?
?
These verses command you as a 'believer' to not betray Allah. Have you betrayed Him by assuming your sins remove you from the fold of faith, thereby neglecting your duties as a believer? What will you do today to strengthen your identity as a believer despite your shortcomings?

Proof That Major Sinners Enter Paradise
Nasafi argues from Quranic promises to guarantee Paradise for major sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi argues from Quranic promises to prove that believers who commit major sins will ultimately enter Paradise.

Core Idea
Major sinners are believers; the Quran promises Paradise to believers, so they will enter.

Having established that faith remains even when a believer commits a major sin, Nasafi now turns to the question of ultimate destiny. He argues that the very same Quranic promises of Paradise that apply to all believers must also apply to those who sin, because they are still believers who have performed righteous deeds. This lesson examines the proof he offers from multiple verses.

Guided Path
1
Recall the established premise
Nasafi begins by reminding us of what has already been proven: faith does not disappear except by outright denial, and it remains even with major sins.
This premise is crucial — without it the argument would not work.
2
Identify the main claim
Nasafi then states his conclusion: such a person will definitely enter Paradise. Note the certainty (la mahala).
3
Examine the evidence
He cites numerous Quranic verses that promise Paradise to those who believe and do righteous deeds. Notice how each verse links faith and good deeds to reward.
He argues that the sinner qualifies because he is a believer and has done many good deeds.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: does this mean the sinner will not be punished at all? Nasafi implies that punishment may occur, but the ultimate end is Paradise, not eternal Hell.
This refutes the view of Kharijites and Mutazilites who said grave sinners are eternally damned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا ثبت أن الإيمان لا يزول بما دون التكذيب وأنه مع ارتكاب الكبيرة باق فنقول : إنه يدخل الجنة لا محالة لما ورد من الوعد للمؤمنين بالثواب في دار الآخرة في كثير من الآيات من نحو قوله تعالى: إِنَّ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ كَانَتْ لَهُمْ جَنَّاتُ الْفِرْدَوْسِ نُزُلاً وصاحب الكبيرة مؤمن وقد عمل صالحات كثيرة إِنَّ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ هُمْ جَنَّاتٌ تَجْرِي مِنْ تَحْتِهَا الْأَنْهَارُ ذَلِكَ الْفَوْزُ الْكَبِيرُ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ أُولَئِكَ هُمْ خَيْرُ الْبَرِيَّةِ وقوله : إِلَّا مَنْ ءَامَنَ وَعَمِلَ صَالِحًا وَأُولَئِكَ لَهُمْ جَزَاءُ النِّعَمِ بِمَا عَمِلُوا وَهُمْ فِي الْغُرُفَاتِ آمِنُونَ وقوله: فَمَنْ يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ وقوله : وَمَنْ يَعْمَلْ مِنَ الصَّالِحَاتِ مِنْ ذَكَرٍ أَوْ أُنْثَى وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ وقوله : مَنْ عَمِلَ سَيِّئَةً فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا وَمَنْ عَمِلَ صَالِحًا مِنْ ذَكَرٍ أَوْ أُنْثَى وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ فَأُولَئِكَ يَدْخُلُونَ الْجَنَّةَ يُرْزَقُونَ فِيهَا بِغَيْرِ حِسَابٍ وقوله : مَنْ جَاءَ بِالْحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ عَشْرُ أَمْثَالِهَا وَمَنْ جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا الآية. في آيات لا تحصى.
Then, when it is established that faith does not disappear by what is less than denial, and that it remains along with the commission of a major sin, we say: He will definitely enter Paradise, because of the promise to the believers of reward in the Hereafter that has come in many verses, such as His saying: 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds, for them will be the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging' – and the perpetrator of a major sin is a believer and has performed many righteous deeds – 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds, for them will be gardens beneath which rivers flow; that is the great attainment.' 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds, they are the best of creation.' And His saying: 'Except those who believe and do righteous deeds, and for them is the reward of blessings for what they did, and they in the upper chambers will be secure.' And His saying: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it.' And His saying: 'And whoever does righteous deeds, whether male or female, while being a believer...' And His saying: 'Whoever does a bad deed will not be recompensed except the like thereof; and whoever does righteous deeds, whether male or female, while being a believer – those will enter Paradise, provided therein without account.' And His saying: 'Whoever comes with a good deed will have ten times the like thereof; and whoever comes with an evil deed will not be recompensed except the like thereof' the verse. In verses without count.
Source pages p. 1051

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the perpetrator of a major sin will inevitably enter Paradise. The author first restates the established premise: faith does not depart due to anything less than outright denial, and it remains even when one commits a major sin. Given that, he asserts that such a person will definitely enter Paradise. The reason provided is the numerous Quranic promises of reward to believers. The author then cites a series of verses that promise Paradise and reward to those who believe and do righteous deeds. He emphasizes that the perpetrator of a major sin is still a believer and has also performed many righteous deeds, so he qualifies for these promises. The verses include: 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds - for them will be the Gardens of Paradise as a lodging'; 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds - for them will be gardens beneath which rivers flow; that is the great attainment'; 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds - they are the best of creation'; 'Except those who believe and do righteous deeds - and for them is the reward of blessings for what they did, and they in the upper chambers will be secure'; 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it'; 'And whoever does righteous deeds, whether male or female, while being a believer...'; 'Whoever does a bad deed will not be recompensed except the like thereof; and whoever does righteous deeds, whether male or female, while being a believer - those will enter Paradise, provided therein without account'; 'Whoever comes with a good deed will have ten times the like thereof; and whoever comes with an evil deed will not be recompensed except the like thereof'. The author concludes that there are countless such verses. The argument relies on the conditional nature of the promises: they are for believers who do good, and the grave sinner is still a believer and has good deeds, so the promise applies. Punishment for sins may still occur, but the ultimate end is Paradise. This refutes the view that the grave sinner will be eternally in Hell (Kharijites, Mutazilites).

Key Terms
جنة (Paradise)
وعد (promise)
ثواب (reward)
صالحات (righteous deeds)
كبيرة (major sin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That major sins cause a believer to be eternally damned to Hell like an unbeliever.
Correction: The passage shows that even though a believer commits major sins, because he still has faith and has done righteous deeds, the Quranic promises of Paradise apply to him, guaranteeing his ultimate entry into Paradise.

Checkpoint

A) Because he will repent before death
B) Because he is still a believer and has performed righteous deeds, and the Quran promises Paradise to believers
C) Because the major sin is automatically forgiven
D) Because Allah does not punish believers

Key Takeaway
Nasafi proves that every believer, even one who commits major sins, will ultimately enter Paradise because the Quranic promises of Paradise apply to all believers who do good deeds, and the sinner is still a believer with good deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage affirms that your good deeds, even if mixed with sins, are not wasted. How does this change the way you view your daily efforts in worship? Will you increase them with hope or slacken with presumption?
?
The Quranic promises are unconditional for believers. Do you truly live as a believer who does good deeds, or do you rely on the label of 'Muslim' while neglecting righteous actions? What one deed will you start doing consistently to make the promise truly yours?

Quranic Promise for the Grave Sinner
Al-Nasafi argues that the grave sinner remains a believer and will enter Paradise, citing Quranic promises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi uses Quranic promises to argue that a grave sinner who remains a believer will ultimately enter Paradise.

Core Idea
Faith alone secures Paradise, even if stained by major sin.

This passage presents the crucial Maturidi argument that the grave sinner's eventual salvation is guaranteed by the Quranic promises to believers. Al-Nasafi establishes that faith persists despite major sin, then invokes numerous verses to show that every believer—including the sinner—is entitled to the reward of Paradise. The lesson unpacks this scriptural reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation slowly. Notice the flow from the general statement to the Quranic citations.
The passage begins with a statement about the People of the Qibla.
2
Identify the key premise
Al-Nasafi asserts that faith does not vanish with anything less than outright denial. This premise is crucial for the argument. Understand why he states this before citing verses.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Look at each quoted verse. They all promise Paradise to believers who do righteous deeds. Note that the grave sinner, despite his sin, is still a believer and has done many righteous deeds (including faith). How does this apply?
Think about how the verses are used as proof.

Whoever dies from the People of the Qibla, and seeking forgiveness for them and asking mercy for them without scrutinizing his state whether he committed a major sin or not. The committer of a major sin, his return is to Paradise without doubt. Commentary
This passage is a scriptural proof. It begins with a thesis: the grave sinner among the People of the Qibla is not to be excommunicated or refused prayer; his final destination is Paradise. The author then states a foundational premise: faith does not disappear with anything less than outright denial (takdhib). Therefore, the grave sinner retains his faith despite committing a major sin. Using this premise, the author argues that the grave sinner will certainly enter Paradise because the Quran contains many verses promising Paradise to believers who do good deeds. The verses are cited: 1) Surah al-Kahf 18:107 mentioning the Garden of Firdaws; 2) Surah al-Buruj 85:11; 3) Surah al-Bayyinah 98:7 calling believers the best of creatures; 4) Surah Saba 34:37; 5) Surah al-Zalzalah 99:7; 6) Surah al-Nisa 4:124; 7) Surah Ghafir 40:40 promising entry to Paradise without account; 8) Surah al-An'am 6:160 promising tenfold reward. The implication is that the grave sinner, despite his sin, is still a believer and has many good deeds (like faith itself and other acts), so the promise applies to him. The author concludes that such verses are countless.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a Muslim who commits a major sin will be eternally punished in Hell.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic promises to believers guarantee Paradise for all who maintain faith, even if they commit major sins.

Knowing that your faith is not nullified by your own major sins, are you complacent about sinning, or does this promise drive you to do more good deeds to secure the reward? Which attitude reflects true gratitude for Allah's mercy?
?
If you were to die tonight with your current sins, would you be confident in the promise of Paradise based solely on your faith? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on Allah's grace versus your own deeds?

Forgiveness for Believers Who Commit Sins
Nasafi argues for forgiveness of believers' sins via divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why believers who commit major sins are not eternally punished and how God's attributes of forgiveness guarantee their eventual pardon.

Core Idea
God's attributes of forgiveness must be actualized; therefore believers' sins are forgivable.

Imam al-Nasafi addresses a critical question: do believers who commit major sins face eternal punishment? He argues that while punishment is possible, verses of threat without mention of eternity leave room for forgiveness. By examining divine attributes and prophetic intercession, he shows that God's mercy prevails for believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical flow from threat verses to divine names.
2
Understand the role of istighfar
Note that angels and prophets seek forgiveness for believers, which implies that believers sometimes deserve punishment. Otherwise, seeking forgiveness would be a request for injustice.
This is a key rebuttal to the idea that believers never deserve punishment.
3
Analyze the divine names
See how God's names (Ghafur, Ghaffar, Afuww) prove that forgiveness is actualized for some sinners. It is impossible for God to be described with attributes that are not realized.
4
Draw the conclusion
Recognize that major sins do not remove faith, because otherwise forgiveness could not be realized. This establishes the Maturidi position on grave sins.
Contrast with Kharijite view that major sins cause disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما ليس بمقرون بذكر الخلود فمن الجائز أن يكون فى المؤمن إذ تعذيب المؤمن بقدر ذنبه ليس بمستحيل في العقل ولا قام دليل شرعي على امتناعه، ووردت به أخبار كثيرة ثم ثبت استغفار الملائكة والأنبياء عليهم السلام للمؤمنين، وكذا المؤمنون أمروا بالاستغفار بعضهم لبعض والاستغفار لمن لا يجز تعذيبه سؤال ترك الظلم وهذا كفر، وكذا مغفرة من لم يستوجب العذاب محال وكذا العفو دل أن من العباد من يستوجب العقوبة ثم الله تعالى يغفر له بفضله ويعفو عنه. يحققه أنه تعالى غافر الذنب، وكذا يسمى غفورًا وغفارا، ورد بهذه الأسامي نصوص الكتاب، وكذا الله تعالى عفو وتسمية الله تعالى بما لا يتحقق منه ومدحه نفسه بما لا يتصور منه محال، ثم بالإجماع ليس ذلك في حق المكذبين ولا الخارجين عن الإسلام، فدل أن ذلك في حق من ارتكب المعصية من غير الاستحلال فكان في دليل أن العبد بالمعصية لا يكفر وبالكبيرة لا يخرج عن الإيمان ليتحقق عفوه والمغفرة له ودليل جواز مغفرة ما دون الكفر إذ لولا ذلك لما تحقق
And as for what is not accompanied by the mention of eternity, it is possible that it pertains to the believer, because the punishment of the believer to the extent of his sin is not impossible in reason, nor has any legal proof been established for its impossibility. Many reports have come about it. Then it is established that the angels and prophets, peace be upon them, seek forgiveness for the believers, and likewise the believers are commanded to seek forgiveness for one another. And seeking forgiveness for one who is not to be punished is a request to leave injustice, and that is disbelief. Likewise, forgiving someone who does not deserve punishment is impossible, and likewise pardon indicates that among the servants are those who deserve punishment, then Allah forgives him by His bounty and pardons him. He verifies that: He is the Forgiver of sin, and likewise He is called Al-Ghafūr (the Oft-Forgiving) and Al-Ghaffār (the All-Forgiving); the texts of the Book have come with these names. And likewise Allah is Al-'Afuww (the Pardoner), and naming Allah with what is not realized from Him and praising Himself with what is inconceivable from Him is impossible. Then by consensus that does not apply to those who deny or those who leave Islam. So it indicates that it applies to the one who commits sin without deeming it permissible. So it was in the proof that the servant does not disbelieve by sin nor leaves faith by a major sin, so that His pardon and forgiveness may be realized for him, and it is proof of the permissibility of forgiveness for what is less than disbelief, for otherwise it would not be realized.
Source pages p. 1054

Commentary
This passage discusses verses of threat that are not accompanied by mention of eternal punishment. The author argues that such verses can apply to believers, because punishing a believer for his sins is rationally possible and not prohibited by any revealed evidence. He supports this with reports about angels and prophets seeking forgiveness for believers, and the command for believers to seek forgiveness for each other. He then argues that seeking forgiveness for someone who is not to be punished would be a request for injustice, which is impossible for God. Therefore, it must be that some believers deserve punishment but are eventually forgiven. He grounds this in God's names: Al-Ghafūr (Oft-Forgiving), Al-Ghaffār (All-Forgiving), and Al-'Afuww (Pardoner). It is impossible for God to be described with attributes that are not realized in action; hence forgiveness must be actualized for some sinners within the Muslim community, excluding disbelievers. This demonstrates that sins (including major sins) do not remove faith, because otherwise forgiveness and pardon could not be realized. Thus, the possibility of forgiveness for sins less than kufr is established.

Correction: The passage argues that while punishment is possible, God's attributes of forgiveness and the practice of istighfar show that believers are ultimately forgiven.

Key Takeaway

?

The author argues that the verses prove a sinner is not a mushrik or munafiq.

The verses prove that a major sinner remains a believer, not an apostate.

This short passage is a direct refutation of the Kharijite and Mu'tazilite positions that equate major sins with disbelief or hypocrisy. Refuting the Kharijite Argument from the Verse of Disobedience
Refutes Kharijite argument using prophets' slips and alternative interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Kharijite interpretation of the verse of disobedience by showing its inconsistency with the treatment of prophets' slips and by offering an alternative interpretation.

Core Idea
The Kharijites cannot consistently claim that disobedience leads to eternal hell without condemning the prophets.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi confronts a key Kharijite proof text—the verse threatening eternal punishment for disobedience. He exposes their inconsistency by asking about the prophets' own slips, and then provides a sound interpretation of the verse that redirects its threat to those who deem sin permissible or to disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the author's line of questioning and the two possible answers the Kharijites might give.
2
Identify the first question
The author asks the Kharijites whether the slips of the prophets were called disobedience. This is the pivot of the refutation. Consider why this question is effective.
3
Understand the dilemma
Trace the two branches of the dilemma. If they say the slips were not disobedience, they contradict the Quran and commit disbelief. If they say they were disobedience, they must then answer whether the prophets deserved eternal hell—which they cannot affirm without kufr or deny without abandoning their proof.
The author forces the Kharijites into a logical corner.
4
Examine the alternative interpretation
The author then offers an alternative reading of the verse: it refers to those who consider sin permissible (istihlal) or to the disbeliever. Look at the three pieces of evidence: the collective noun 'limits', the phrase 'the most wretched', and 'prepared for the disbelievers'.
This shows the verse does not automatically apply to every major sinner.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this refutation protects the status of believers who commit major sins without deeming them permissible, and how it upholds the consistency of Quranic interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعلق الخوارج بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يَعْصِ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ (٢) الآية قلنا لهم: ما تقولون في زلات الأنبياء عليهم السلام أنها هل كانت تسمى عصيانا؟ فإن قالوا: لا. كذبوا الله تعالى في قوله: ﴿ وَعَصَى عَادَمُ رَبَّهُ، فَغَوَى﴾ ونسبوه إلى الظلم حيث عاتب داود وغيره من الأنبياء على ما وجد منهم من الزلات وأبطلوا مغفرته لداود بقوله : ﴿ فَغَفَرْنَا لَهُ ذَلِكَ﴾ وذلك كفر . وإن قالوا: كان ذلك منهم عصيانًا قيل: فهل كفروا واستحقوا الخلود في النار ؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. كفروا وإن قالوا: لا. أبطلوا دليلهم. ثم الآية مصروفة إلى الاستحلال على أن في الآية دليلاً أنها وردت في الكافر لأنه قال : ويتعد حدوده والحدود اسم جمع والمؤمن لا يتعدى جميع حدود الله، وقوله: لا يَصْلَهَا إِلَّا الأَشْقَى أي على طريق الإصالة، وكذا قوله: ﴿أُعِدَّتْ للكفرين﴾ على طريق الإصالة والله أعلم.
And the Kharijites clung to His statement, the Exalted: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger...' the verse. We said to them: What do you say about the slips (zallat) of the prophets, peace be upon them — were those called disobedience? If they say: No. They lie against Allah the Exalted in His statement: 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord, so he erred' and they attribute injustice to Him where He rebuked David and other prophets for what occurred from them of slips, and they nullify His forgiveness for David by His statement: 'So We forgave him that' — and that is disbelief. And if they say: That was disobedience from them, it is said: Did they then disbelieve and deserve eternity in the Fire? If they say: Yes. They disbelieve. And if they say: No. They nullify their proof. Then the verse is turned to [the meaning of] considering it permissible (al-istihlal), and also in the verse is proof that it came concerning the disbeliever, because He said: '...and transgresses His limits' and 'limits' is a collective noun, and the believer does not transgress all of Allah's limits; and His statement: 'None shall enter it except the most wretched' — that is, in the primary sense; and likewise His statement: 'Prepared for the disbelievers' — in the primary sense. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 1056

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument refuting the Kharijite interpretation of the verse 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger...' (Q 4:14). The Kharijites used this verse to claim that any major sinner (ṣāḥib al-kabīra) is eternally in Hell. The author (al-Nasafī) responds by asking them about the slips (zallāt) of the prophets — were those considered disobedience? If the Kharijites deny that prophets' slips were disobedience, they contradict the Qur'an which explicitly calls Adam's action 'disobedience' (ʿiṣyān) and states that Allah rebuked David and others for their slips, and also they would nullify Allah's forgiveness of David. This would be disbelief (kufr). If they admit that the prophets' slips were disobedience, then the author asks: did those prophets thereby disbelieve and deserve eternal punishment in Hell? If the Kharijites say yes, they commit disbelief by declaring prophets disbelievers. If they say no, they undermine their own proof, because they now admit that disobedience does not necessarily entail eternity in Hell. The author then provides an alternative interpretation of the verse: it refers to those who consider sin permissible (istiḥlāl) or to the disbeliever, because the verse uses the collective noun 'limits' (ḥudūd) — a believer does not transgress all of Allah's limits — and because the verse says 'None shall enter it except the most wretched' and 'Prepared for the disbelievers', which indicates primary application to the disbeliever. Thus the Kharijite argument is refuted.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author shows the verse must be understood in light of other Quranic principles and actually refers to those who deem sin permissible or to disbelievers, not every major sinner.

Checkpoint

A) Did the prophets commit major sins?
B) Were the slips of the prophets called disobedience?
C) Did the prophets believe in Allah?
D) Do you accept the authority of the Qur'an?

Key Takeaway
The verse of disobedience does not automatically condemn every sinner; it must be interpreted in light of the Qur'an's treatment of prophets and its own context.

Refuting That the Major Sinner Is a Polytheist or Hypocrite
Refutes the claim that a major sinner becomes a polytheist or hypocrite using the story of Yusuf's brothers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a major sinner does not become a polytheist or hypocrite, based on the definitions of shirk and nifaq and the permanence of tasdiq (affirmation of faith).

Core Idea
Major sins do not negate faith; only denial of truth does.

This passage tackles a key challenge to the Maturidi view that faith does not increase or decrease by actions. Some argued that major sinners are like polytheists or hypocrites. The author refutes this by clarifying what true polytheism and hypocrisy are, and by showing that the major sinner retains the core of faith—affirmation (tasdiq). The story of Yusuf's brothers powerfully illustrates that even grave sins do not make one a hypocrite.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument flow through you.
Notice how the author uses narrative as evidence.
2
Identify the definitions of shirk and nifaq
The passage defines polytheism (shirk) as belief in a partner in worship or creation, and hypocrisy (nifaq) as having a specific essence opposed to faith. See how the major sinner does not meet these definitions.
The two types of shirk are explicitly mentioned.
3
Examine the example of Yusuf's brothers
The author narrates that Yusuf's brothers lied, betrayed trust, and broke promises—actions that are signs of a hypocrite—yet they are not considered hypocrites. This shows that such actions are signs, not the essence of hypocrisy.
This example is a powerful refutation of those who equate actions with hypocrisy.
4
Understand the permanence of tasdiq
The author concludes that the major sinner remains a believer because he has affirmation (tasdiq). The major sin does not negate that affirmation, and the evil names he deserves do not contradict his faith.
Tasdiq is the core of faith; actions do not affect its presence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبتلك الآيات يبطل قول من يجعله مشركًا أو منافقا، فإن الله تعالى أبقى الإيمان وكذا الإشراك اعتقاد شريك الله تعالى إما في العبادة كمشركي العرب، وإما التخليق كالمجوس، وصاحب الكبيرة لا يعتقد ذلك ولا يشرك البتة بعبادة ربه أحدا، وقد بينا أيضا مائيَّة النفاق وأن صاحب الكبيرة ليس بداخل في حده بل هو على الضد من حال المنافق، وما ذكر من الحديث فذلك علامة المنافق في الأغلب فإما أن تكون هي بنفسها نفاقًا فلا، وقد روى أن عطاء لما مذهب الحسن سمع قال: قولوا له إن أخوة يوسف ائتمنوا فخانوا حيث ألقوه في غياهب الجب، وحدثوا فكذبوا بقولهم: أكله الذئب، ووعدوا بقولهم: وإنا له لحافظون فأخلفوا هل صاروا بذلك منافقين ؟ فقيل للحسن ذلك، فقال: صدق عطاء ورجع عن ذلك على أن الحديث يمكن حمله على الاستحلال، والله الموفق. ثم إن هذا الرجل كان مؤمنًا لما معه من التصديق وبالكبيرة ما انعدم، وما حدث له من الأسماء الخبيثة كالفاسق والظالم والفاجر وغيرها، فتلك أسماء يستحقها بأفعال ليست بمنافية للإيمان فثبوتها لا يكون منافيًا لكونه مؤمنًا، ثم لما لم يصر كافراً لانعدام التكذيب لم لم يبق مؤمنًا ومعه التصديق ولم يوجد منه ما يضاده
And by those verses is invalidated the statement of whoever makes him [the major sinner] a polytheist or a hypocrite. For Allah the Exalted preserved faith. Likewise polytheism (al-ishrāk) is the belief in a partner with Allah the Exalted, either in worship like the polytheists of the Arabs, or in creation like the Magians, and the committer of a major sin (ṣāḥib al-kabīra) does not believe that and does not associate anyone at all in worship of his Lord. And we have also explained the nature (mā'iyya) of hypocrisy (al-nifāq) and that the committer of a major sin is not included within its definition; rather he is opposite to the condition of the hypocrite. What has been mentioned of the hadith, that is a sign of the hypocrite in most cases; but that they themselves are hypocrisy, no. And it has been narrated that ʿAṭā' when he heard the school of al-Ḥasan said: Say to him: The brothers of Yusuf entrusted themselves and then betrayed when they threw him into the dark pit, and they spoke and lied by their saying: 'The wolf ate him,' and they promised by their saying: 'And we are surely his guardians,' and then they broke their promise — did they become hypocrites by that? So that was said to al-Ḥasan, and he said: ʿAṭā' spoke the truth, and he retracted that opinion, though the hadith can be interpreted as referring to considering it permissible (al-istihlāl). And Allah is the Grantor of success. Then this man was a believer because of the affirmation (al-taṣdīq) that was with him, and by the major sin he did not cease [to be a believer]. And what occurred to him of evil names like 'sinner' (al-fāsiq), 'wrongdoer' (al-ẓālim), 'immoral' (al-fājir) and others — those are names he deserves for actions that are not contradictory to faith, so their being established is not contradictory to his being a believer. Then since he did not become a disbeliever due to the absence of denial (al-takdhīb), why should he not remain a believer while affirmation is with him and no opposite to it has occurred from him?
Source pages p. 1056

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that a major sinner becomes a polytheist (mushrik) or a hypocrite (munāfiq). The author begins by stating that the previous verses (from the earlier lesson) already invalidate that claim. He then explains the nature of polytheism: it is either associating partners in worship (like the Arabs) or in creation (like the Magians). The major sinner does not hold such beliefs. Next, he recalls that the nature (mā'iyya) of hypocrisy has been explained, and the major sinner does not fall under its definition; indeed he is the opposite of a hypocrite. As for the hadith that lists signs of a hypocrite (e.g., lying, betraying trust, breaking promises), those are signs in most cases, but the actions themselves are not necessarily hypocrisy. To illustrate, the author recounts a story: ʿAṭā' ibn Abī Rabāḥ (or Wāṣil ibn ʿAṭā') heard al-Ḥasan al-Baṣrī's view and asked: 'Were the brothers of Yūsuf, who betrayed trust, lied, and broke promises, thereby hypocrites?' Al-Ḥasan is reported to have agreed with ʿAṭā' and retracted his earlier opinion, though the hadith can be explained as referring to those who consider such acts permissible (istiḥlāl). The author then concludes that the man (the major sinner) remains a believer because he possesses affirmation (taṣdīq) of faith. The major sin does not negate that affirmation. The evil names like 'sinner', 'wrongdoer', 'immoral' are deserved for his actions, but those actions are not contradictory to faith itself. Since he has not committed denial (takdhīb), he remains a believer. Thus, the idea that major sin removes faith entirely is refuted.

Key Terms
الإشراك
النفاق
علامة المنافق
التصديق
الأسماء الخبيثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A major sinner becomes a polytheist or a hypocrite because of his sins.
Correction: Polytheism is defined as belief in partners to Allah; hypocrisy is defined as inner disbelief while outwardly showing faith. A major sinner does not hold such beliefs; he has affirmation of faith (tasdiq). Sins do not negate faith; only denial (takdhīb) does.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to argue that actions like lying and breaking promises do not necessarily make one a hypocrite?
A) The example of Adam's disobedience
B) The example of the brothers of Yusuf
C) The example of the Magians
D) The example of the polytheists of Arabia

Key Takeaway
A major sinner remains a believer as long as he has affirmation of faith (tasdiq); his sinful actions do not make him a polytheist or hypocrite in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you ever worry that your faith has weakened or disappeared? How does this lesson's emphasis on tasdiq (affirmation) challenge you to hold onto faith while still taking sin seriously? What specific action will you take next time you sin to reaffirm your tasdiq without excusing the sin?

Are you more concerned about your label (being called a believer) or your actual condition (the reality of your tasdiq and actions)? What does your response reveal about your heart, and what will you do to align your inner affirmation with your outward life?
