The Major Sinner is Neither a Mushrik nor a Munafiq
Refutes that the major sinner is a mushrik or munafiq via the story of 'Ata'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a major sinner is not necessarily a hypocrite or polytheist, and how the story of Yusuf's brothers illustrates this key principle.

Core Idea
Major sins do not make one a hypocrite; actions can be signs without being the reality.

This lesson addresses a common misconception: that committing major sins makes one a hypocrite (munafiq). The author refutes this by defining shirk and nifaq clearly, then using the powerful example of Yusuf's brothers (who committed betrayal, lying, and breaking promises) to show that such actions are not equivalent to hypocrisy itself. By the end, you'll see how the early scholarly debate between Hasan al-Basri and 'Ata' resolved this issue.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definitions
The passage first clarifies that shirk is belief in a partner to God, and the major sinner does not hold such belief. It also reminds that nifaq has a specific essence that excludes the major sinner.
Notice the author uses previous explanations of nifaq from earlier in the book.
2
Analyze the hadith sign
The author addresses a hadith that lists signs of hypocrisy (like lying, betraying trust). He explains these are signs in most cases, not the reality of nifaq itself.
This is a key distinction: a sign is not same as the thing itself.
3
Learn from 'Ata''s challenge
'Ata' uses the story of Yusuf's brothers—who betrayed trust, lied, and broke promises—to argue that if such actions made a person a hypocrite, then the brothers would be hypocrites, which is not the case. Hasan accepts this reasoning and retracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان وكذا الإشراك اعتقاد شريك الله تعالى إما في العبادة كمشركي العرب، وإما التخليق كالمجوس، وصاحب الكبيرة لا يعتقد ذلك ولا يشرك البتة بعبادة ربه أحدا، وقد بينا أيضا مائية النفاق وأن صاحب الكبيرة ليس بداخل في حده بل هو على الضد من حال المنافق، وما ذكر من الحديث فذلك علامة المنافق في الأغلب فإما أن تكون هي بنفسها نفاقا فلا، وقد روى أن عطاء لما مذهب الحسن سمع قال: قولوا له إن أخوة يوسف ائتمنوا فخانوا حيث ألقوه في غياهب الجب، وحدثوا فكذبوا بقولهم: أكله الذئب، ووعدوا بقولهم: وإنا له لحافظون فأخلفوا هل صاروا بذلك منافقين ؟ فقيل للحسن ذلك، فقال: صدق عطاء ورجع عن ذلك على أن الحديث يمكن حمله على الاستحلال، والله الموفق.
Faith: and likewise shirk is belief in a partner to God Almighty either in worship like the mushriks of the Arabs, or in creation like the Magians. And the committer of a major sin does not believe that, and does not associate any partner at all with his Lord in worship. And we have already explained the essence of hypocrisy (nifaq) and that the committer of a major sin does not enter into its definition; rather he is the opposite of the state of the hypocrite. And what has been mentioned of the hadith, that is a sign of the hypocrite in most cases; as for it being itself hypocrisy, no. And it has been narrated that 'Ata' when he heard the school of Hasan said: 'Tell him: The brothers of Yusuf were entrusted and they betrayed when they threw him into the darkness of the well, and they spoke and lied by their saying: "the wolf ate him", and they promised by their saying: "and we are indeed his guardians" but they broke (their promise); did they become hypocrites by that?' So it was said to Hasan that, and he said: ''Ata' has spoken truth' and he retracted from that, upon the basis that the hadith can be interpreted as (referring to) deeming (sin) permissible (istihlal). And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 1057

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that a major sinner becomes a hypocrite (munafiq). The author begins by stating that shirk is belief in a partner to God, either in worship (as the Arab polytheists) or in creation (as the Magians). The major sinner does not believe this and does not associate partners. Then he reminds that the essence of nifaq has been explained, and the major sinner is not included in its definition; rather he is the opposite. Then he addresses the hadith that lists signs of hypocrisy (e.g., lying, betraying trust). He explains that these are usually signs of a hypocrite, but they do not constitute hypocrisy in themselves. To support this, he recounts an incident where 'Ata' (Wāṣil ibn 'Aṭā') challenged Hasan al-Basrī: if the signs of hypocrisy were enough to label someone a hypocrite, then the brothers of Yusuf—who betrayed trust, lied about the wolf, and broke their promise—would have been hypocrites. Upon hearing this, Hasan accepted 'Ata''s reasoning and retracted his earlier view. The author adds that the hadith can be interpreted as referring to those who deem sins permissible (istihlāl), not to every sinner. Thus, the major sinner remains a believer, not a hypocrite.

Correction: The author clarifies that these signs are usually indicators but not the reality of nifaq. The example of Yusuf's brothers shows that even major sins like betrayal and lying do not make one a hypocrite.

The Major Sinner Remains a Believer Despite Sins
Explains that a person with major sins remains a believer, and that verses of punishment may apply to mustahillin or are subject to God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a person who commits major sins remains a believer, and how verses of punishment are interpreted to support this view.

Core Idea
Committing a major sin does not remove faith; it merely earns blameworthy names.

The Maturidi school firmly upholds that a believer who commits major sins does not lose faith. This lesson explains that inner affirmation (tasdiq) remains intact despite sinful acts, and that the sinful names acquired (like fasiq) are not contradictory to faith. Furthermore, we will see how threatening verses that lack mention of eternal punishment are interpreted flexibly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the author's two-part argument: first about the believer's status, then about the scriptural objections.
Pay attention to the key terms: tasdiq, takdhib, mustahill.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the Arabic terms tasdiq, takdhib, kabira, fasiq, mustahill, and khulud. Understand how they are used in the argument.
These terms are central to the Maturidi position on faith and major sins.
3
Understand the core argument
Note that the author argues: the major sinner has tasdiq (inner affirmation); the major sin does not remove that tasdiq. The names like fasiq are for actions that do not contradict faith, so they don't negate belief.
4
Examine the response to scriptural objections
The author addresses verses that seem to threaten punishment. He says they either refer to mustahillin (who are actually disbelievers) or, if not, the punishment is subject to God's will—He may forgive or punish.
This is a key move to reconcile threatening verses with the view that major sinners remain believers.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this position matters for understanding faith and God's mercy. It emphasizes that faith is defined by inner affirmation, not by perfect action.
This aligns with the Maturidi emphasis on the distinction between iman and actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن هذا الرجل كان مؤمنا لما معه من التصديق وبالكبيرة ما انعدم، وما حدث له من الأسماء الخبيثة كالفاسق والظالم والفاجر وغيرها، فتلك أسماء يستحقها بأفعال ليست بمنافية للإيمان فثبوتها لا يكون منافيا لكونه مؤمنا، ثم لما لم يصر كافرا لانعدام التكذيب لم لم يبق مؤمنا ومعه التصديق ولم يوجد منه ما يضاده وقد خرج الجواب عن تعلقهم بقوله تعالى : ( الذين يأكلون أموال اليتامى ظلما ) وبغيره من الآيات التي ليس فيها ذكر الخلود أن ذلك يجوز أن تكون واردة في المستحلين، ويجوز أن تكون واردة في غير المستحلين، ويكون ذلك معلقا بمشيئة الله تعالى على ما قررنا.
Then this person was a believer due to the tasdiq (affirmation) that he has, and by the major sin it did not cease to exist. And what occurred to him of evil names such as fasiq (sinner), zalim (wrongdoer), fajir (immoral), and others, these are names he deserves for actions that are not contradictory to faith, so their being established is not contradictory to his being a believer. Then, since he did not become a disbeliever due to the absence of takdhib (denial), why would he not remain a believer when he has tasdiq and nothing that contradicts it has occurred from him? And the answer to their claim based on His saying 'Indeed, those who consume the property of orphans unjustly' and other verses that do not mention eternal punishment (khulud) is that it is possible that these are revealed regarding those who deem (such acts) permissible (mustahillin), and it is possible that they are revealed regarding those who do not deem them permissible, and that is dependent on the will of God Almighty, as we have established.
Source pages p. 1057

Commentary
This passage continues the defense of the position that a major sinner remains a believer. The author argues that such a person is a believer because he possesses tasdiq (inner affirmation of faith), and the major sin does not annihilate that tasdiq. He acquires new names due to his sinful actions—like fasiq, zalim, fajir—but these names are for actions that do not negate faith. So having these names does not contradict his being a believer. Then he poses a rhetorical question: since the person did not become a disbeliever because there is no takdhib (denial), why would he not remain a believer when he still has tasdiq and no opposing thing has occurred? The author then addresses the Mu'tazilite objection based on verses that threaten punishment for sinners, such as the verse about consuming orphans' property. He points out that these verses do not explicitly mention eternal punishment (khulud). Therefore, they could be interpreted in two ways: either they refer to those who deem the sin permissible (mustahillin), who are in fact disbelievers, or if they refer to non-mustahillin, the punishment is subject to God's will—He may punish or forgive. Thus, the verses do not prove that the major sinner will be eternally punished or that he has left faith.

التكذيب
الكبيرة
الفاسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People who commit major sins are no longer believers.
Correction: According to the Maturidi school, they remain believers because they still have inner affirmation (tasdiq); the sinful actions do not remove faith.

Checkpoint

B) They may refer to those who deem sins permissible or are subject to God's will.
C) They are abrogated.
D) They only apply to disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
A believer who commits major sins remains a believer because inner affirmation (tasdiq) is not negated by sinful acts; threatening verses are interpreted as conditional or referring to disbelievers.

The Absolute Sinner and the Sinning Believer
Distinguishes fasiq mutlaq from fasiq muti' and refutes a Mu'tazilite proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between an absolute sinner (fasiq mutlaq) and a sinning believer, and why the term 'fasiq' in certain verses refers specifically to the disbeliever.

Core Idea
An absolute sinner is one with no obedience at all; a believer is never an absolute sinner.

The Mu'tazila argued that a major sinner is like a disbeliever, citing a Qur'anic verse equating believers and sinners. The author responds by carefully defining two types of sinners: the absolute sinner (the disbeliever) and the sinning obedient believer. This distinction is crucial for understanding God's justice and the fate of believers who commit major sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two sinners
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on how the author distinguishes between fasiq mutlaq and fasiq muti'.
Notice the author uses the term 'fasiq mutlaq' for the disbeliever, not for the major sinner.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection
The author responds to the Mu'tazilite use of the verse 'Afaman kana mu'minan...' Identify their claim and the author's counter.
3
Identify the proof from context
The author provides a proof from the context of the verse. Find that proof and see how it strengthens the distinction.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters for the doctrine of faith: a believer who commits a major sin is still obedient in faith, and thus not an absolute sinner.
This counters the Kharijite and Mu'tazilite view of eternal punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ هناك فاسق مطلق وفاسق مطيع] كان وتعلقهم بقوله تعالى: أَفَمَن كَانَ مُؤْمِنًا كَمَن كَانَ فَاسِقًا لَا يَسْتَوُونَ قلنا : الآية وردت في الفاسق المطلق، والمؤمن ليس بفاسق مطلق بل هو فاسق بما ارتكب من المعصية مطيع بما معه من الإيمان والطاعات والفاسق المطلق من فاسقا من جميع الوجوه بحيث ليست معه طاعة ولا هو موصوف بها بوجه، فأما من كان مطيعا بما هو رأس كل طاعة وبما لا يحصى من الطاعات فليس هو بفاسق مطلق، وقد وقع في أفواه من لا علم لهم بهذا الباب أن الفاسق المطلق هو مرتكب الكبيرة، ولكن ذلك باطل وقع فيما بين الناس بتلقين المعتزلة. والدليل على أن المراد بالفاسق المطلق الكافر سياق الآية وهو قوله تعالى: وَقِيلَ لَهُمْ ذُوقُوا عَذَابَ النَّارِ الَّذِي كُنتُم بِهِ تُكَذِّبُونَ، وهذا وصف الكفار دون صاحب الكبيرة. التكلم بلفظ العموم والمراد به الخصوص سائغ عند أهل اللغة]: وما يزعمون أن الإخبار بتعذيب مرتكبي الذنوب وردت مطلقة عامة ولو أخرج منها شيء كان خلفًا، وذلك كذب إذ الكذب هو الإخبار عن المخبر لا على ما هو به والكذب على الله تعالى محال ولا وجه إلى القول بتخصيص من عفى عنه وغفر له في الآية، لأن صيغة العموم متى وردت متعرية عن دليل الخصوص كانت دلالة على إرادة المتكلم جميع أفراد ما يتناوله اللفظ كأنه نص على كل فرد
[There is an absolute sinner (fasiq mutlaq) and a sinning obedient (fasiq muti').] And their attachment to His saying: 'Is he who is a believer like he who is a sinner? They are not equal.' We say: The verse was revealed about the absolute sinner. And the believer is not an absolute sinner; rather he is a sinner by what he committed of disobedience, obedient by what he has of faith and acts of obedience. And the absolute sinner is the one who is a sinner from all aspects such that there is no obedience with him, nor is he described with it in any way. As for the one who is obedient by what is the head of every obedience and by countless acts of obedience, he is not an absolute sinner. And it has fallen into the mouths of those who have no knowledge of this subject that the absolute sinner is the committer of a major sin, but that is false; it arose among people by instillation from the Mu'tazila. And the proof that what is meant by the absolute sinner is the disbeliever is the context of the verse, namely His saying: 'And it was said to them: Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny.' And this is a description of the disbelievers, not the committer of a major sin. Speaking with a general expression intending specific meaning is permissible among the people of language. And what they claim that the reports about punishment of sinners have come absolutely generally, and if anything is excluded from them it would be a lie — that is falsehood. For lying is reporting about the matter not as it is, and lying about God Almighty is impossible. And there is no way to say that specifying those whom He has pardoned and forgiven in the verse is (unacceptable), because when a general expression comes devoid of evidence for specification, it indicates the speaker's intention of all individuals that the word encompasses, as if it were a text about every individual.
Source pages p. 1058

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues: the definition of fasiq mutlaq (absolute sinner) and the permissibility of specifying general expressions. The author first responds to the Mu'tazilite use of the verse 'Afaman kana mu'minan kaman kana fasiqan' (Is he who is a believer like he who is a sinner?). The Mu'tazila argue that this verse equates the major sinner with the disbeliever in being subject to eternal punishment. The author counters by distinguishing between an absolute sinner and a sinning believer. The absolute sinner is one who has no obedience at all, i.e., the disbeliever. The believer who commits a major sin is a sinner in some respects but obedient in others, especially in faith, which is the head of all obedience. Therefore, he is not an absolute sinner. The author asserts that the common claim that the absolute sinner is the one who commits a major sin is false and originates from Mu'tazilite indoctrination. He then provides contextual proof from the verse itself: the following words 'Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny' refers to those who deny, i.e., disbelievers, not to the major sinner who affirms faith. Then the author moves to a second point: the objection that verses threatening punishment are general and that any exclusion would be a lie. He counters by explaining that it is permissible in language to use a general term while intending a specific meaning (takhsis). Lying is defined as reporting contrary to reality. If God intends to specify some individuals, then specifying does not constitute lying. Moreover, when a general expression comes without an indication of specification, it implies all individuals. But when there is evidence for specification (like God's will or other verses), then the general can be limited. So the verses about punishment may be specified to apply only to those who deserve it without injustice.

Key Terms
الفاسق المطلق
الفاسق المطيع
السياق
التكذيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a believer who commits a major sin is an absolute sinner like a disbeliever.
Correction: A believer has faith and is obedient in that respect, so he is not an absolute sinner.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the proof that 'fasiq mutlaq' in the verse refers to the disbeliever?
A) The context of the verse mentions denial.
B) The author says so without proof.
C) The Mu'tazila agree.
D) The verse explicitly says 'disbeliever'.

Key Takeaway
An absolute sinner is a disbeliever without any obedience; a believing sinner retains faith, the greatest act of obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have committed a major sin while still believing in Allah, do you ever feel that you have become like a disbeliever? How does this lesson's distinction between fasiq muti' and fasiq mutlaq change that feeling? What does it demand of you right now regarding your hope and your repentance?
?
When you see someone else trapped in a major sin, do you secretly write them off as hopeless? Based on this passage, what is the truth about their faith? Will you now change how you treat them?
?
The Mu'tazilites propagated a harsh view that made believers despair. Are there any beliefs in your heart—perhaps from your environment—that make Allah's mercy seem distant? Will you reject them today, using the proof from context that the absolute sinner is only the denier?

Specification of General Expressions in the Qur'an
Argues that specifying general expressions is linguistically valid and not a lie.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how general expressions in the Qur'an can be specified without constituting a lie, and how this principle applies to verses of punishment.

Core Idea
Using a general term to mean a specific group is permissible and does not involve lying.

The Mu'tazila objected that if God's threats of punishment were general, excluding anyone would be a lie. The author defends the orthodox view that specification (takhsis) of general expressions is a well-established linguistic practice; it is not lying because the speaker intends the specific meaning. This principle safeguards God's justice and mercy while upholding the truth of His speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on specification
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the author's definition of lying and his argument about general expressions.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection
They claim that if any sinner is exempted from a general threat, then God would be lying. Identify this claim in the text.
3
Grasp the author's definition of lying
The author defines lying as reporting contrary to reality. If God intends to specify some individuals, then specifying does not contradict reality.
4
Learn the rule for general expressions
The author explains that a general expression without evidence for specification indicates all individuals. But when there is evidence (like God's will), specification is allowed.
5
Apply to the case of punishment verses
Understand that verses threatening punishment can be specified to apply only to those who truly deserve it, without injustice or falsehood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ هناك فاسق مطلق وفاسق مطيع] كان وتعلقهم بقوله تعالى: أَفَمَن كَانَ مُؤْمِنًا كَمَن كَانَ فَاسِقًا لَا يَسْتَوُونَ قلنا : الآية وردت في الفاسق المطلق، والمؤمن ليس بفاسق مطلق بل هو فاسق بما ارتكب من المعصية مطيع بما معه من الإيمان والطاعات والفاسق المطلق من فاسقا من جميع الوجوه بحيث ليست معه طاعة ولا هو موصوف بها بوجه، فأما من كان مطيعا بما هو رأس كل طاعة وبما لا يحصى من الطاعات فليس هو بفاسق مطلق، وقد وقع في أفواه من لا علم لهم بهذا الباب أن الفاسق المطلق هو مرتكب الكبيرة، ولكن ذلك باطل وقع فيما بين الناس بتلقين المعتزلة. والدليل على أن المراد بالفاسق المطلق الكافر سياق الآية وهو قوله تعالى: وَقِيلَ لَهُمْ ذُوقُوا عَذَابَ النَّارِ الَّذِي كُنتُم بِهِ تُكَذِّبُونَ، وهذا وصف الكفار دون صاحب الكبيرة. التكلم بلفظ العموم والمراد به الخصوص سائغ عند أهل اللغة]: وما يزعمون أن الإخبار بتعذيب مرتكبي الذنوب وردت مطلقة عامة ولو أخرج منها شيء كان خلفًا، وذلك كذب إذ الكذب هو الإخبار عن المخبر لا على ما هو به والكذب على الله تعالى محال ولا وجه إلى القول بتخصيص من عفى عنه وغفر له في الآية، لأن صيغة العموم متى وردت متعرية عن دليل الخصوص كانت دلالة على إرادة المتكلم جميع أفراد ما يتناوله اللفظ كأنه نص على كل فرد
[There is an absolute sinner (fasiq mutlaq) and a sinning obedient (fasiq muti').] And their attachment to His saying: 'Is he who is a believer like he who is a sinner? They are not equal.' We say: The verse was revealed about the absolute sinner. And the believer is not an absolute sinner; rather he is a sinner by what he committed of disobedience, obedient by what he has of faith and acts of obedience. And the absolute sinner is the one who is a sinner from all aspects such that there is no obedience with him, nor is he described with it in any way. As for the one who is obedient by what is the head of every obedience and by countless acts of obedience, he is not an absolute sinner. And it has fallen into the mouths of those who have no knowledge of this subject that the absolute sinner is the committer of a major sin, but that is false; it arose among people by instillation from the Mu'tazila. And the proof that what is meant by the absolute sinner is the disbeliever is the context of the verse, namely His saying: 'And it was said to them: Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny.' And this is a description of the disbelievers, not the committer of a major sin. Speaking with a general expression intending specific meaning is permissible among the people of language. And what they claim that the reports about punishment of sinners have come absolutely generally, and if anything is excluded from them it would be a lie — that is falsehood. For lying is reporting about the matter not as it is, and lying about God Almighty is impossible. And there is no way to say that specifying those whom He has pardoned and forgiven in the verse is (unacceptable), because when a general expression comes devoid of evidence for specification, it indicates the speaker's intention of all individuals that the word encompasses, as if it were a text about every individual.
Source pages p. 1058

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues: the definition of fasiq mutlaq (absolute sinner) and the permissibility of specifying general expressions. The author first responds to the Mu'tazilite use of the verse 'Afaman kana mu'minan kaman kana fasiqan' (Is he who is a believer like he who is a sinner?). The Mu'tazila argue that this verse equates the major sinner with the disbeliever in being subject to eternal punishment. The author counters by distinguishing between an absolute sinner and a sinning believer. The absolute sinner is one who has no obedience at all, i.e., the disbeliever. The believer who commits a major sin is a sinner in some respects but obedient in others, especially in faith, which is the head of all obedience. Therefore, he is not an absolute sinner. The author asserts that the common claim that the absolute sinner is the one who commits a major sin is false and originates from Mu'tazilite indoctrination. He then provides contextual proof from the verse itself: the following words 'Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny' refers to those who deny, i.e., disbelievers, not to the major sinner who affirms faith. Then the author moves to a second point: the objection that verses threatening punishment are general and that any exclusion would be a lie. He counters by explaining that it is permissible in language to use a general term while intending a specific meaning (takhsis). Lying is defined as reporting contrary to reality. If God intends to specify some individuals, then specifying does not constitute lying. Moreover, when a general expression comes without an indication of specification, it implies all individuals. But when there is evidence for specification (like God's will or other verses), then the general can be limited. So the verses about punishment may be specified to apply only to those who deserve it without injustice.

Key Terms
العموم
الخصوص
التخصيص
الكذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God would be lying if He exempts some sinners from a general threat.
Correction: Lying is reporting contrary to what is intended; if God intends specification, the statement is true.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the definition of lying?
A) Saying something that is not true.
B) Reporting about the matter not as it is.
C) Using a general expression.
D) Excluding someone from a general statement.

Key Takeaway
General expressions can be specified based on intention and evidence; doing so does not constitute lying.

Defining the Absolute Sinner and Refuting the Mu'tazila
Al-Nasafi defines fasiq mutlaq and refutes the Mu'tazilite proof from Quran 32:18.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between an absolute sinner (fasiq mutlaq) and a believer who commits sins, and how the Mu'tazilite interpretation of Quran 32:18 is refuted.

Core Idea
An absolute sinner is a disbeliever, not a grave sinner with faith.

Al-Nasafi clarifies a key term in Islamic theology: the 'absolute sinner.' The Mu'tazila used Quran 32:18 to argue that grave sinners are not believers. But careful analysis shows the verse refers to disbelievers, not to Muslims who commit sins. This lesson unpacks that distinction and the evidence from the Quranic context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full English translation. Notice the flow of argument.
The author begins by quoting the Mu'tazilite proof.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite proof
Find the verse the Mu'tazila used (32:18) and understand their claim that a sinner is not a believer.
3
Understand the definition of fasiq mutlaq
See how Al-Nasafi defines the absolute sinner as one with no obedience at all, contrasting with a believer who has faith and obedience.
Focus on the phrase 'from all aspects' (min jami' al-wujuh).
4
Analyze the contextual argument
Read the next verse that mentions punishment of the Fire. Al-Nasafi uses this to show the verse is about disbelievers.
5
Recognize the common mistake
Note the widespread confusion between absolute sinner and grave sinner, which Al-Nasafi attributes to Mu'tazilite teaching.

وتعلقهم بقوله تعالى: أَفَمَن كَانَ مُؤْمِنًا كَمَن كَانَ فَاسِقَا لَا يَسْتَوُونَ قلنا: الآية وردت في الفاسق المطلق، والمؤمن ليس بفاسق مطلق بل هو فاسق بما ارتكب من المعصية مطيع بما معه من الإيمان والطاعات والفاسق المطلق من فاسقا من جميع الوجوه بحيث ليست معه طاعة ولا هو موصوف بها بوجه، فأما من كان مطيعا بما هو رأس كل طاعة وبما لا يحصى من الطاعات فليس هو بفاسق مطلق، وقد وقع في أفواه من لا علم لهم بهذا الباب أن الفاسق المطلق هو مرتكب الكبيرة، ولكن ذلك باطل وقع فيما بين الناس بتلقين المعتزلة. والدليل على أن المراد بالفاسق المطلق الكافر سياق الآية وهو قوله تعالى: وَقِيلَ لَهُمْ ذُوقُوا عَذَابَ النَّارِ الَّذِي كُنتُم بِهِ تُكَذِّبُونَ، وهذا وصف الكفار دون صاحب الكبيرة.
And their attachment to His statement: 'Is he who is a believer like he who is a sinner? They are not equal.' We said: The verse came concerning the absolute sinner, and the believer is not an absolute sinner, but rather he is a sinner by what he committed of disobedience, obedient by what he has of faith and acts of obedience. The absolute sinner is one who is sinful from all aspects such that there is no obedience with him nor is he described by it in any way. As for one who is obedient by what is the head of every obedience and by countless acts of obedience, he is not an absolute sinner. And it has fallen into the mouths of those who have no knowledge of this subject that the absolute sinner is the perpetrator of the major sin, but that is false and has spread among the people by the teaching of the Mu'tazila. The evidence that what is meant by absolute sinner is the disbeliever is the context of the verse, which is His statement: 'And it will be said to them: Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny.' And this is a description of the disbelievers, not the perpetrator of the major sin.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite interpretation of Quran 32:18. The author begins by quoting the Mu'tazilite proof that the verse distinguishes believers and sinners, and the Mu'tazila claim that a grave sinner is a fasiq (sinner) and thus not a believer. The author responds by explaining that the verse speaks of the 'absolute sinner' (fasiq mutlaq), which means a person who is sinful in every aspect with no obedience whatsoever, like a disbeliever. The believer, even if he commits a major sin, still has faith and obedience, so he is not an absolute sinner. The author then notes that the term 'absolute sinner' has been misunderstood by common people due to Mu'tazilite influence. The contextual evidence from the verse—mention of the punishment of the Fire and denial—shows that the verse is about disbelievers, not grave sinners. Thus the Mu'tazilite proof fails.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The absolute sinner (fasiq mutlaq) is understood as anyone who commits a major sin.
Correction: The absolute sinner is actually the disbeliever, who has no obedience at all. A believer who commits a major sin is not an absolute sinner because he still has faith and obedience.

Necessity of Distinguishing Disbelief from Other Sins
The author explains why a distinction between disbelief and other sins is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theology requires a distinction between disbelief (kufr) and other sins regarding forgiveness and eternal punishment, based on Quranic teachings and rational necessity.

Core Idea
Divine justice demands a clear distinction between kufr and lesser sins in the afterlife.

In this passage, the author addresses a key theological question: must we distinguish between disbelief and other sins when considering forgiveness and punishment? He presents two positions: one that sees no need for distinction, and another—held by the Maturidi school—that insists on it based on divine wisdom and Quranic verses. Let's explore this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two theological positions
Read the first part of the passage and note that some theologians (People of Hadith who allow pardoning disbelievers) see no need for a distinction, while the author's companions (Maturidis) argue it is necessary. Understand why the first position collapses the distinction.
The first view treats all sins similarly regarding forgiveness.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence for distinction
The author cites three Quranic verses that reject treating believers and sinners equally. Read each verse and reflect on how they imply that justice requires different outcomes.
Verses: 38:28, 45:21, 68:35-36.
3
Trace the logical argument for afterlife distinction
Note the reasoning: since there is no perfect separation between the righteous and wicked in this world, the afterlife must provide the distinction. This makes the distinction between kufr and other sins necessary.
4
Understand the doctrinal consequence
Conclude that disbelief cannot be pardoned and leads to eternal punishment, while lesser sins may be forgiven and do not result in eternal punishment. This preserves divine wisdom.
This distinction is essential for the Maturidi position on divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا حاجة إلى الفرق بين الكفر وغيره من الذنوب في حق جواز العفو والتعذيب والتخليد في النار وغير التخليد لمن يرى رأي متكلمي أهل الحديث في جواز العفو عن الكافر. فأما من يقول من أصحابنا أن لا بد في الحكمة من التفرقة بين المسييء والمحسن على ما استبعد الله تعالى من التسوية بينهما بقوله: أَمْ نَجْعَلُ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ كَالْمُفْسِدِينَ فِي الْأَرْضِ أَمْ نَجْعَلُ الْمُتَّقِينَ كَالْفُجَّارِ وقوله: أَمْ حَسِبَ الَّذِينَ اجْتَرَحُوا السَّيِّئَاتِ أَن نَّجْعَلَهُمْ كَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ سَوَاءً مَّحْيَاهُمْ وَمَمَاتُهُمْ سَاءَ مَا يَحْكُمُونَ وقوله تعالى: أَفَنَجْعَلُ الْمُسْلِمِينَ كَالْمُجْرِمِينَ مَا لَكُمْ كَيْفَ تَحْكُمُونَ. ثم لا تفرقة بين الفريقين في الدنيا فلابد من أن يفرق بينهما في الآخرة فهؤلاء يحتاجون إلى الفرق بين الكفر وبين ما دونه من الذنوب في جواز العفو عما دون الكفر وامتناعه فيه، وثبوت تخليد العذاب فيه، وسقوطه عما دونه.
Then there is no need for a distinction between disbelief and other sins with respect to the permissibility of pardon, punishment, eternal punishment in the Fire, and non-eternal punishment for one who holds the opinion of the speculative theologians of the People of Hadith regarding the permissibility of pardoning the disbeliever. But as for those among our companions who say: it is necessary in wisdom to make a distinction between the wrongdoer and the doer of good, as Allah Almighty deemed equality between them unlikely in His saying: "Or shall We treat those who believe and do righteous deeds like those who spread corruption in the earth? Or shall We treat the righteous like the wicked?" And His saying: "Or do those who commit evil deeds think that We shall make them like those who believe and do righteous deeds, equal in life and death? Evil is that they judge." And His saying: "Shall We treat the Muslims like the criminals? What is the matter with you? How do you judge?" Then there is no distinction between the two groups in this world, so there must be a distinction between them in the Hereafter. So these people need to make a distinction between disbelief and what is below it of sins regarding the permissibility of pardon for what is below disbelief and its impossibility for it, and the affirmation of eternal punishment for it, and its non-occurrence for what is below it.
Source pages p. 1070

Commentary
This passage clarifies the necessity of distinguishing between disbelief (al-kufr) and other sins regarding forgiveness and punishment. The author first notes that for those who hold the view of the speculative theologians of the People of Hadith (ahl al-hadith) that a disbeliever can be pardoned, no such distinction is needed — all sins are treated similarly. However, for those among the author's school (Ashabuna, i.e., the Maturidis) who affirm that wisdom (hikma) demands a distinction between the wrongdoer (al-musi') and the doer of good (al-muhsin), it becomes essential to differentiate. The author cites three Quranic verses that reject equality between believers and sinners, implying that justice requires different treatment. Since in this world there is no perfect separation (the righteous and wicked coexist), the afterlife must establish the distinction. Consequently, one must distinguish between disbelief (which cannot be forgiven and leads to eternal punishment) and lesser sins (which can be forgiven and do not result in eternal punishment). This distinction upholds divine wisdom.

ذنوب (dhunub) - sins
عفو ('afw) - pardon
تعذيب (ta'dhib) - punishment
تخليد (takhliyid) - eternal punishment
حكمة (hikma) - wisdom
تفرقة (tafriqa) - distinction
المسيء (al-musi') - wrongdoer
المحسن (al-muhsin) - doer of good
أهل الحديث (ahl al-hadith) - People of Hadith

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sins are equal in terms of forgiveness and punishment; there is no special status for disbelief.
Correction: The text argues that disbelief (kufr) is fundamentally different from other sins: it cannot be pardoned and leads to eternal punishment, while lesser sins may be forgiven and do not result in eternal punishment.

D) The author's own companions.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between disbelief and other sins is necessary to uphold divine wisdom and justice, as affirmed by the Quran and reason.

First Argument: Good Deeds of the Major Sinner
First argument: good deeds of major sinner vs disbeliever

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that distinguishes the punishment of kufr from major sins: the presence of good deeds in the major sinner.

Core Idea
Faithful sinners have good deeds; disbelievers have none.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by explaining a fundamental difference between major sins (except shirk) and disbelief: the person committing a major sin still possesses acts of obedience, whereas the disbeliever lacks all good. This argument highlights the justice of God in differentiating punishments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the contrast between the major sinner and the disbeliever.
2
Identify the claim
The author claims that the major sinner, at the time of sin, is still acquiring acts of obedience (fear, hope, trust). Locate this in the text.
3
Notice the contrast
The passage contrasts the major sinner's good deeds with the disbeliever's lack of any good. How does this difference lead to different punishments?
4
Reflect on divine justice
Why is it unjust to punish the major sinner eternally? The author argues that the good deeds outweigh the sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق أن ما من أحد يعصي الله بنوع من الكبائر دون الشرك إلا وهو لوقت العصيان مكتسب للطاعات من خوف عقابه ورجاء رحمته والثقة بكرمه، وذلك خيرات لو قوبل بها ما ارتكب من الخلاف بغلبة شهوة وقهر غضب أو نحو ذلك لترجح ما كان فيه من خير على ما كان منه من شر، فلا يجوز أن يحرم نفع الخير ويوجب له عقوبة الشر، وليس مع من يكفر بالله تعالى ويشرك به معنى يستحق اسم الخير لأنه يكذبه وينكر أمره ونهيه. والثاني: أن الكفر مذهب يعتقد للأبد إذ المذاهب تعتقد للأبد فعلى ذلك عقوبته من سائر الكبائر لأوقات وهو عند غلبة شهوة لا للأبد بل في عقيدة كل من ارتكبها العزم على أن يتوب عنها لو أملي له في مهلة وفسح في أجله، فعلى ذلك عقوبتها. والثالث: أن الكفر نفسه لا يحتمل الإباحة ورفع الحرمة عنه فعلى ذلك عقوبته في الحكمة لا تحتمل الارتفاع والعفو عنه بخلاف سائر المآثم. والرابع: أن الله تعالى أحسن إلى صاحب الكبيرة في الدين في الوقت الذي جفاه في أن جعل حقه أعظم في قلبه من الدارين، وأنبياءه ورسله عليهم السلام أجل في صدره من أن تحتمل نفسه الاستحقاق بشعرة من شعورهم أو الركون إلى أحد أعدائه من فيما قد اختاره وآثره من الخلاف، فلا يجوز في الحكمة أن يضيع هذا الإحسان بجفوة يعلم أن قدرها من الذنوب لا يبلغ جزءًا مما لا يحصى من مننه وإحسانه بخلاف الكافر والله الموفق.
And the difference is that no one disobeys God with a type of major sin except shirk except that at the time of disobedience he is acquiring acts of obedience from fear of His punishment and hope for His mercy and trust in His generosity, and that is good deeds which if compared to what he committed of opposition due to overwhelming desire or overpowering anger or the like, the good in him would outweigh the evil, so it is not permissible to deprive him of the benefit of the good and impose on him the punishment of the evil. And the one who disbelieves in God Almighty and associates partners with Him has no meaning deserving the name of good because he denies Him and rejects His command and prohibition. Second: that disbelief is a creed held forever, for creeds are held forever, so its punishment is [eternal] while other major sins are for [specific] times, and it [the sin] is under the influence of overwhelming desire, not forever, but in the belief of everyone who commits it there is the resolve to repent from it if he is granted respite and his term is extended, so accordingly is its punishment. Third: that disbelief itself does not admit permissibility and removal of prohibition from it, so accordingly its punishment in wisdom does not admit removal and pardon, unlike other sins. Fourth: that God Almighty has done good to the possessor of major sin in religion at the time He was harsh with him, in that He made His right greater in his heart than the two worlds, and His prophets and messengers, peace be upon them, are more venerable in his heart than that his soul could bear deserving a hair of their hairs or inclining to any of His enemies from what he has chosen and preferred of opposition, so it is not permissible in wisdom that this goodness be wasted by a harshness whose magnitude from sins does not reach a part of what cannot be counted of His favors and goodness, unlike the disbeliever. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1071

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments that establish the difference between the punishment of disbelief (kufr) and that of other major sins. The author (Imam al-Nasafi) begins by stating the difference (الفرق). The first argument is that a person who commits a major sin other than shirk still has acts of obedience at the time of disobedience (fear, hope, trust). Their good deeds would outweigh the evil if compared, so it is unjust to deprive them of the benefit of their good and punish them for the evil. In contrast, the disbeliever has no good deeds because he denies God. The second argument: disbelief is a permanent creed, so its punishment is eternal, whereas other major sins are temporary acts committed under passion, and the sinner intends to repent. The third argument: disbelief itself cannot be made permissible or removed from prohibition, so its punishment cannot be lifted, unlike other sins which can be forgiven. The fourth argument: God has bestowed great favors upon the major sinner (like making belief in Him and love of prophets great in his heart), while the disbeliever lacks these, so it would be unwise to let the major sinner's good be wasted by a small sin. This shows the justice and wisdom of God's judgment.

Key Terms
الكبائر
الشرك
الكفر
العقوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any sin, including major sins, deserves eternal punishment.
Correction: The author explains that major sinners still have good deeds and intend to repent, so their punishment is not eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why does a major sinner not deserve eternal punishment?
A) Because he never actually sins.
B) Because he has good deeds that outweigh the sin.
C) Because God is not just.
D) Because his sin is not really a sin.

Key Takeaway
The major sinner's good deeds prevent eternal punishment, unlike the disbeliever who has none.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you still find within yourself fear of God's punishment, hope for His mercy, and trust in His generosity? If not, what does that say about the state of your faith according to this passage?
?
This passage argues that the good deeds of a faithful sinner outweigh his evil, so he does not deserve eternal punishment. Do you truly believe that your own good deeds outweigh your evil? What evidence from your life would you present to God if He were to weigh your deeds today?
?
The disbeliever is said to have no meaning deserving the name of good because he denies God. How does this reality make you appreciate the immense value of your faith, even when you sin? Do you treat your faith as a treasure that protects you from eternal loss, or do you take it for granted?

Second Argument: Disbelief as a Permanent Creed
Second argument: permanence of disbelief vs temporariness of sin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument: disbelief is a permanent creed, while major sins are temporary with intention to repent.

Core Idea
Disbelief is a lasting creed; sins are fleeting with repentance planned.

The second argument shifts focus to the nature of disbelief as a creed. Unlike major sins, which are committed under passion and with an intention to repent, disbelief is a chosen worldview held permanently. Thus, the punishment matches the permanence of the belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Pay attention to the words 'creed' and 'forever'.
2
Compare duration
The author contrasts the eternal nature of disbelief with the temporary nature of major sins. Find the phrases that indicate this.
3
Note the intention to repent
The passage says that the major sinner intends to repent. Why does this affect the punishment?
4
Reflect on proportion
The punishment is proportional to the deed. How does this argument support eternal punishment for disbelief?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق أن ما من أحد يعصي الله بنوع من الكبائر دون الشرك إلا وهو لوقت العصيان مكتسب للطاعات من خوف عقابه ورجاء رحمته والثقة بكرمه، وذلك خيرات لو قوبل بها ما ارتكب من الخلاف بغلبة شهوة وقهر غضب أو نحو ذلك لترجح ما كان فيه من خير على ما كان منه من شر، فلا يجوز أن يحرم نفع الخير ويوجب له عقوبة الشر، وليس مع من يكفر بالله تعالى ويشرك به معنى يستحق اسم الخير لأنه يكذبه وينكر أمره ونهيه. والثاني: أن الكفر مذهب يعتقد للأبد إذ المذاهب تعتقد للأبد فعلى ذلك عقوبته من سائر الكبائر لأوقات وهو عند غلبة شهوة لا للأبد بل في عقيدة كل من ارتكبها العزم على أن يتوب عنها لو أملي له في مهلة وفسح في أجله، فعلى ذلك عقوبتها. والثالث: أن الكفر نفسه لا يحتمل الإباحة ورفع الحرمة عنه فعلى ذلك عقوبته في الحكمة لا تحتمل الارتفاع والعفو عنه بخلاف سائر المآثم. والرابع: أن الله تعالى أحسن إلى صاحب الكبيرة في الدين في الوقت الذي جفاه في أن جعل حقه أعظم في قلبه من الدارين، وأنبياءه ورسله عليهم السلام أجل في صدره من أن تحتمل نفسه الاستحقاق بشعرة من شعورهم أو الركون إلى أحد أعدائه من فيما قد اختاره وآثره من الخلاف، فلا يجوز في الحكمة أن يضيع هذا الإحسان بجفوة يعلم أن قدرها من الذنوب لا يبلغ جزءًا مما لا يحصى من مننه وإحسانه بخلاف الكافر والله الموفق.
And the difference is that no one disobeys God with a type of major sin except shirk except that at the time of disobedience he is acquiring acts of obedience from fear of His punishment and hope for His mercy and trust in His generosity, and that is good deeds which if compared to what he committed of opposition due to overwhelming desire or overpowering anger or the like, the good in him would outweigh the evil, so it is not permissible to deprive him of the benefit of the good and impose on him the punishment of the evil. And the one who disbelieves in God Almighty and associates partners with Him has no meaning deserving the name of good because he denies Him and rejects His command and prohibition. Second: that disbelief is a creed held forever, for creeds are held forever, so its punishment is [eternal] while other major sins are for [specific] times, and it [the sin] is under the influence of overwhelming desire, not forever, but in the belief of everyone who commits it there is the resolve to repent from it if he is granted respite and his term is extended, so accordingly is its punishment. Third: that disbelief itself does not admit permissibility and removal of prohibition from it, so accordingly its punishment in wisdom does not admit removal and pardon, unlike other sins. Fourth: that God Almighty has done good to the possessor of major sin in religion at the time He was harsh with him, in that He made His right greater in his heart than the two worlds, and His prophets and messengers, peace be upon them, are more venerable in his heart than that his soul could bear deserving a hair of their hairs or inclining to any of His enemies from what he has chosen and preferred of opposition, so it is not permissible in wisdom that this goodness be wasted by a harshness whose magnitude from sins does not reach a part of what cannot be counted of His favors and goodness, unlike the disbeliever. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1071

?

Third Argument: Disbelief Cannot Be Made Permissible
Third argument: intrinsic impermissibility of disbelief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument: disbelief is inherently impermissible and cannot be made lawful, so its punishment cannot be lifted.

Core Idea
Disbelief can never be permissible; thus its punishment is irrevocable.

The third argument highlights an intrinsic property of disbelief: it cannot be declared permissible or removed from prohibition. Consequently, the punishment for disbelief is also eternal and cannot be pardoned, unlike other major sins which can be forgiven.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the short Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the words 'permissibility' and 'removal of prohibition'.
2
Understand the property
The author says disbelief itself does not admit being made permissible. Why is that? Reflect on the nature of disbelief.
3
Apply to punishment
The author connects this property to punishment: if the act cannot be made permissible, its punishment cannot be removed. See the logical link.
4
Contrast with other sins
Other sins can be forgiven or their prohibition lifted? The author mentions 'unlike other sins'. How does this contrast work?

Fourth Argument: God's Favors on the Major Sinner
Fourth argument: God's favors on the major sinner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument: God has bestowed immense favors on the major sinner (love for Him and the prophets), which outweigh his sin, so it would be unwise to punish him eternally.

Core Idea
God's favors on a believer outweigh any sin, so eternal punishment is unjust.

The fourth argument appeals to the concept of divine wisdom and grace. Despite the major sin, God has granted the sinner a deep love for Him and His prophets, which is a great blessing. To punish such a person eternally for a sin that is minuscule compared to these favors would be unwise. This contrasts with the disbeliever who lacks these favors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the description of God's favors: making His right greater in the heart, and venerating the prophets.
2
Identify the favors
List the favors God has given the major sinner mentioned in the passage: greater love for God, reverence for prophets.
3
Compare the sin to the favors
The author says the sin does not reach a part of the favors. Why does this make eternal punishment unwise?
4
Contrast with the disbeliever
The disbeliever lacks these favors. How does this contrast support the difference in punishment?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
5 min
Why Mu'tazili and Khariji Major Sinners Are Deemed Disbelievers
The author argues that Mu'tazila and Khawarij who commit major sins become kafir due to their belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twofold reasoning behind the Maturidi position that Mu'tazili and Khariji major sinners are considered disbelievers.

Core Idea
Believing you are a disbeliever makes you a disbeliever.

This passage presents a specific case within the broader discussion of major sins: why do the Mu'tazila and Khawarij become disbelievers when they commit a major sin? The author provides two interlocking proofs, one from the Qur'an and one from the logic of self-conception. Read carefully to see how despair and self-belief combine.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic source text once, then the English translation. Note that the passage begins with 'ثم نقول' (Then we say), indicating it is part of an ongoing argument.
Try to follow the two arguments separately.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage contains two distinct arguments: first, the Qur'anic proof about despairing of God's mercy; second, the logical proof about believing oneself to be a disbeliever. Locate each.
The first proof uses two Qur'anic verses; the second is a self-contained logical statement.
3
Understand the conjunction of sin and belief
Notice that the author does not say the sin alone causes disbelief. Rather, it is the sin combined with the doctrinal belief that the sin removes one from faith. This is the key distinction.
4
Reflect on the principle of self-definition
The second argument is subtle: if someone believes he is not a believer, then he truly is not a believer. Consider how inner conviction defines one's faith status according to this reasoning.
This principle is crucial for understanding Maturidi anthropology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: إن صاحب الكبيرة إذا كان معتزليًا أو خارجيًا يكفر لأنه بارتكابه ييأس من روح الله ويقنط من رحمة الله تعالى والله تعالى يقول: إِنَّهُ لَا يَأْيَسُ مِن رَّوْحِ اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْقَوْمُ الْكَافِرُونَ وقال: وَمَن يَقْنَطُ مِن رَّحْمَةِ رَبِّهِ إِلَّا الضَّالُونَ ولأنه لما ارتكب الكبيرة مع اعتقاد أنه يكفر أو يخرج عن الإيمان صار بارتكابه معتقدا أنه كافر أو خارج عن الإيمان، ومن اعتقد أنه ليس بمؤمن لا يكون مؤمنًا وكذا من اعتقد أنه كافر، والله الموفق.
Then we say: the possessor of a major sin, if he is a Mu'tazili or a Khariji, becomes a disbeliever because by committing it he despairs of God's relief and loses hope of God's mercy, and God Almighty says: 'Indeed, no one despairs of the relief of God except the disbelieving people' and said: 'And who despairs of the mercy of his Lord except the astray?' And because when he commits the major sin with the belief that it makes a disbeliever or removes him from faith, he becomes by committing it believing that he is a disbeliever or removed from faith. And whoever believes that he is not a believer is not a believer, and likewise whoever believes that he is a disbeliever [is a disbeliever]. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1072

Commentary
This passage explains why a Mu'tazili or Khariji who commits a major sin is considered a kafir. The proof consists of two parts. First, they despair of God's mercy, which is characteristic of disbelievers according to the Qur'an. Second, and more fundamentally, they believe that by committing the major sin they become kafir or outside of faith. Since they hold that belief, they are indeed what they believe themselves to be: a person who believes he is not a believer is not a believer. This is based on the principle that inner conviction defines one's faith status.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person becomes a disbeliever simply by committing a major sin, regardless of their beliefs.
Correction: The author clarifies that it is not the sin alone, but the conjunction of the sin with the belief that the sin causes disbelief, that leads to the judgment of kufr.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does a Mu'tazili who commits a major sin become a disbeliever?
A) Because he commits the sin itself.
B) Because he believes that committing it makes him a disbeliever.
C) Because he does not repent.
D) Because he rejects all prophets.

Key Takeaway
A person who commits a major sin while believing it to be an act of disbelief becomes a disbeliever because he confirms that status upon himself.

?

?
If you believe that a certain sin makes you a disbeliever, what does that belief actually do to your iman according to this lesson? What step will you take to align your belief with the truth of Allah's mercy?

Why Intercession Does Not Encourage Sin
Nasafi refutes that intercession emboldens sin using a counter-argument about repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's two-fold refutation of the claim that intercession encourages sin, and why intercession actually prevents despair.

Core Idea
Intercession does not embolden sin; it removes despair that leads to disbelief.

Critics argue that believing in intercession gives people a license to sin without fear of punishment. Nasafi responds by turning the argument against them: if intercession emboldens, then the Mu'tazili doctrine that God must accept repentance is even more emboldening. Moreover, he shows that intercession does not remove accountability because no one can be certain of receiving it, and it actually prevents the dangerous despair that can lead to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation to understand the overall argument.
2
Identify the two parts of the refutation
Notice first the counter-argument about repentance, then the explanation that intercession does not embolden because of uncertainty.
The refutation has a tu quoque structure: turning the opponent's own doctrine against them.
3
Consider the role of despair
Nasafi points out that intercession actually prevents despair and hopelessness, which can lead to disbelief. Think about why despair is dangerous spiritually.
The Arabic terms يأس and قنوط are key.
4
Reflect on accountability
Ask yourself: Does the uncertainty of receiving intercession make a sinner more cautious or less? How does this compare to the certainty of being able to repent?
This helps reconcile intercession with divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أن إثبات الشفاعة تجرئة على الذنوب. قلنا: وفي إيجاب قبول التوبة عندكم على الله تعالى التجرئة أكثر، ثم نقول : ليس فيه تجرئة لأن كل من ارتكب مأثمًا لا يدري بطريق اليقين أنه ينال شفاعة لا محالة، وعنده أنه يقدر على التوبة لا محالة، وذلك صحيح فهذا أولى بل فيه دفع اليأس والقنوط الموقعين لصاحبهما في الكفر، والله الموفق.
And what they claim: that affirming intercession is an emboldening to sins. We said: And in the obligation of accepting repentance upon God (according to you) there is more emboldening. Then we say: There is no emboldening in it, because everyone who commits a sin does not know with certainty that he will undoubtedly obtain intercession, while he knows that he is undoubtedly able to repent, and that is correct. So this is more appropriate; rather, in it is the removal of despair and hopelessness which land their possessor in disbelief. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1078

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that affirming intercession (shafa'ah) encourages people to sin. The author offers a two-fold response. First, he points out that the Mu'tazila themselves hold that God must accept repentance; if anything, that doctrine encourages sin even more, because one can always repent. Second, he explains that intercession does not encourage sin because a sinner never knows with certainty that he will receive intercession, whereas he knows he is always able to repent. Thus, the hope of repentance is a stronger motivation to avoid sin than hope for intercession. Moreover, intercession actually prevents despair (ya's) and hopelessness (qunoot), which can lead to disbelief, so it serves a beneficial purpose.

Key Terms
شفاعة
تجرئة
توبة
يأس
قنوط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intercession gives people a license to sin without any consequences.
Correction: Nasafi argues that a sinner cannot be certain of receiving intercession, so they fear punishment; additionally, intercession actually prevents despair that would lead to disbelief.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main counter-argument against the claim that intercession encourages sin?
A) Intercession is conditional on righteous deeds.
B) The Mu'tazili belief in obligatory acceptance of repentance is more encouraging of sin.
C) Intercession only applies to minor sins.
D) The prophets do not intercede for persistent sinners.

Key Takeaway
Intercession does not embolden sin; instead, it removes despair and does not give certainty of salvation, preserving accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believed that intercession is uncertain, would you still delay repentance? If not, what would it take for you to treat every sin as urgently requiring immediate tawbah?

Two Classical Definitions of Iman
Nasafi reports two classical definitions of iman and their proponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major classical definitions of iman (faith) and the scholars associated with each.

Core Idea
Faith is defined either as heart+tongue+actions or heart+tongue alone.

Imam Nasafi begins his discussion on faith by acknowledging the multitude of opinions, then presents two principal views. One view includes knowledge, confession, and actions; the other limits faith to knowledge and confession. This lesson will explore these definitions and their proponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the detailed list of scholars for the first view.
2
Identify the two views
The passage presents two definitions: one includes action with limbs, the other excludes it. Note how each is introduced and which scholars are cited.
The second view is associated with specific sects.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters. Does including works in the definition affect how we understand salvation or religious practice?
This debate has deep theological implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[الكلام في الإيمان] الفصل السادس اختلف الناس فيما يقع عليه اسم الإيمان اختلافا لا وجه لذكر ذلك ولا سبيل إليه لكثرة ما فيه من الأقاويل، فنذكر من ذلك جملاً على طريق الاختصار لئلا يخلو كتابنا عن الكلام فيه مقتدين بمن تقدم من السلف رحمهم الله. فنقول : من الناس من زعم أن الإيمان هو المعرفة بالقلب والإقرار باللسان والعمل بالأركان، وحكى هذا عن مالك والشافعي والأوزاعي وأهل المدينة وأهل الظاهر، أئمة الحديث كأحمد بن حنبل وإسحاق بن راهوية ومن المتكلمين منهم عن وجميع الحارث بن أسد المحاسبي وأبي العباس القلانسي وابن علي الثقفي. ومن الناس من زعم أن الإيمان يكون بالقلب واللسان دون غيرهما من الجوارح وإليه يذهب الشمرية والنجارية والغيلانية وحكى هذا عن كثير
[Discussion on Faith] Chapter Six. People differed over what the name 'faith' applies to, a difference that there is no point in mentioning nor way to do so due to the multitude of opinions in it. So we will mention some of that summarily, so that our book is not devoid of discussion on it, following those who preceded from the Salaf, may God have mercy on them. We say: Some people claimed that faith is knowledge in the heart, confession with the tongue, and action with the limbs. And this is reported from Malik, al-Shafi'i, al-Awza'i, the people of Medina, the people of al-Zahir, the imams of hadith like Ahmad ibn Hanbal and Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, and among the mutakallimun, from them and all of al-Harith ibn Asad al-Muhasibi, Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, and Ibn Ali al-Thaqafi. And some people claimed that faith is by the heart and the tongue only, without the other limbs. This is held by al-Shimriyya, al-Najjariyya, and al-Ghailaniyya, and this is reported from many.
Source pages p. 1081

Commentary
This passage is a classification of two major views on the definition of iman (faith). The author, Imam Nasafi, begins by noting the great diversity of opinions on what constitutes iman, but states that he will only mention a summary to avoid excessive length and to follow the methodology of the Salaf. He then presents the first view: iman consists of three components: knowledge (ma'rifa) in the heart, confession (iqrar) with the tongue, and action (amal) with the limbs (jowarih). This comprehensive definition is attributed to prominent scholars including Malik, Shafi'i, Awza'i, the people of Medina, the people of al-Zahir, hadith authorities like Ahmad ibn Hanbal and Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, and some mutakallimun such as al-Muhasibi, al-Qalanisi, and al-Thaqafi. The second view limits iman to the heart and tongue, excluding physical actions. This position is associated with the sects al-Shimriyya, al-Najjariyya, and al-Ghailaniyya, and is reported to be held by many others. The passage thus sets up a key theological debate about whether works are part of the definition of faith or merely its fruits.

المعرفة
الإقرار
العمل
الجوارح
الشمرية
النجارية
الغيلانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the first view excludes proponents of the second, but the passage merely reports both as existing opinions.
Correction: The passage is reporting two different schools of thought, not claiming exclusivity.

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge in the heart, confession with the tongue, and action with the limbs
B) Knowledge in the heart and confession with the tongue only
C) Action with the limbs and confession with the tongue only
D) Knowledge in the heart and action with the limbs only

Key Takeaway
The classical debate on iman centres on whether actions are part of faith or merely its evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The first view requires actions as part of faith. Think of an action you have been negligent in. Does that mean your iman is incomplete? What will you do about it starting today?
?
Both views agree that ma'rifa (knowledge) in the heart is essential. How deep is your personal knowledge of Allah? What one step can you take this week to increase it?

Three Proofs that Iman is Solely Tasdiq
Three linguistic and scriptural proofs that iman is solely tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proofs from language and the Quran that establish iman as solely tasdiq (affirmation of the heart) and not inclusive of actions.

Core Idea
Iman is tasdiq: language, opposites, and Quran all confirm it.

This lesson presents three compelling proofs that iman (faith) is fundamentally tasdiq (affirmation of the heart). The first proof examines the linguistic opposite of iman—kufr, which means denial—implying iman must be affirmation. The second proof relies on the consensus of Arabic speakers who use 'iman' to mean tasdiq. The third proof shows how the Quran consistently distinguishes iman from righteous deeds like prayer and zakat. Together, these proofs establish iman as a distinct, inner act of the heart.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the three distinct sections where the author says 'يحققه' (what establishes it).
The passage uses repetitive structure to separate the proofs.
2
Identify the first proof: from opposites
The first proof is based on the opposition between iman and kufr. Since kufr is denial (takdhib), iman must be affirmation (tasdiq). See how the author quotes Quran 2:256 to support this.
This is a logical argument from linguistic opposites.
3
Identify the second proof: from language
The second proof appeals to the people of language (ahl al-lisan) who define iman as tasdiq. The author argues that changing this meaning would undermine language itself.
This is an argument from linguistic convention.
4
Identify the third proof: from Quranic usage
The third proof shows that the Quran distinguishes iman from actions like prayer and zakat. Verses like 9:18 and many others treat iman as separate from deeds, using 'if you are believers' as a condition for commands.
This shows iman is not the same as righteous deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدلة على أن الإيمان هو التصديق: يحققه أن ضد الإيمان هو الكفر ، والكفر هو التكذيب والجحود، وإن كان يسمى به غيره على المجاز فكذا الإيمان يحققه أن الله تعالى قابل الكفر بالإيمان فقال: {فَمَن يَكْفُرْ بِالطَّاغُوتِ وَيُؤْمِنُ بِاللَّهِ} ثم المراد منها التكذيب والتصديق لا غير فدل أن الإيمان ذلك. يحققه أن الإيمان معروف أنه عند أهل اللسان التصديق لا غير فمن جعله لغير التصديق فقد صرف الاسم عن المفهوم في اللغة إلى غير المفهوم ولو جاز ذلك لجاز في كل اسم لغوي، وفيه إبطال اللسان وتعطيل اللغة وذلك محال. يدل عليه أن الله تعالى فرق بين الإيمان وبين كل عباده بالاسم المعقول على ما فرق بين العبادات بالأسماء المعقول لها على ما قال: {إِنَّمَا يَعْمُرُ مَسَجِدَ اللَّهِ مَنْ آمَنَ بِاللَّهِ وَالْيَوْمِ الْآخِرِ وَأَقَامَ الصَّلَوٰةَ وَآتَى الزَّكَوةَ} وكذا قال في مواضع {إِنَّ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ} وكذا أمر بغير شيء ثم قال: {إِن كُنتُم مُّؤْمِنِينَ} نحو قوله تعالى {وَأَصْلِحُوا ذَاتَ بَيْنِكُمْ} الآية وقوله: {وَذَرُوا مَا بَقِيَ مِنَ الرِّبوا} وقوله: {وَلَا تَهِنُوا وَلَا تَحْزَنُوا وَأَنتُمُ الْأَعْلَوْنَ إِن كُنتُم مُّؤْمِنِينَ} ولو لم يكن الإيمان معروفًا عند كل منهم وما به ثبت الاسم لكان يكون ذلك شرطا غير مفيد. وكذا خاطب الله
The proofs that iman is tasdiq: What establishes it is that the opposite of iman is kufr, and kufr is takdhib (denial) and juhud (rejection), though it may be applied to other things metaphorically; similarly, iman [is tasdiq]. What establishes it is that Allah, exalted, paired kufr with iman, saying: 'So whoever disbelieves in taghut and believes in Allah...' Then what is meant there is denial and affirmation only; so it indicates that iman is that. What establishes it is that iman is known according to the people of language (ahl al-lisan) to be nothing other than tasdiq. So whoever makes it something other than tasdiq has diverted the name from its linguistic meaning to something else. If that were permitted, it would be permitted for every linguistic name, and that would invalidate language and disable speech, which is impossible. What indicates it is that Allah distinguished between iman and all His acts of worship by the known name, just as He distinguished between acts of worship by their known names, as He said: 'Only those who inhabit the mosques of Allah are those who believe in Allah and the Last Day and establish prayer and give zakat.' Likewise He said in places: 'Indeed those who believe and do righteous deeds.' And likewise He commands something else and then says: 'If you are believers' as in His words: 'And make peace between yourselves' and His words: 'And leave what remains of usury' and His words: 'Do not weaken and do not grieve, for you will be superior if you are believers.' If iman were not known to each of them and that by which the name is established, then such a condition would be of no use. Similarly, Allah addressed...
Source pages p. 1084

Commentary
This passage provides three proofs that iman is tasdiq. The first proof is from opposites: just as the opposite of iman is kufr, and kufr linguistically means takdhib (denial), so iman must be tasdiq (affirmation). This is supported by Quran 2:256 where Allah contrasts kufr and iman, implying the intended meanings are denial and affirmation. The second proof is from language (ahl al-lisan): the Arabic language defines iman as tasdiq; anyone who assigns a different meaning is distorting language, which is impossible. The third proof is from the Quranic usage: Allah consistently distinguishes iman from actions like prayer and zakat, as seen in verses such as 9:18 and many others. In these verses, iman is mentioned separately from good deeds, indicating that iman is a distinct concept from actions. The author argues that if iman were not a well-known concept independently, then using 'if you are believers' as a condition for commands would be meaningless. Thus, the linguistic and scriptural evidence confirms that iman is solely affirmation in the heart.

الكفر
التكذيب
الجحود
أهل اللسان
اللغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Iman includes righteous actions as part of its definition.
Correction: The passage proves through language, opposites, and Quranic usage that iman is solely tasdiq (affirmation of the heart), distinct from physical deeds.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that iman is tasdiq according to the passage?
A) The opposite of iman, kufr, is deniel, so iman must be affirmation.
B) The people of language use the word iman for actions.
C) The Quranic command 'establish prayer' is part of iman.
D) The hypocrites were considered believers.

Key Takeaway
Iman is purely affirmation of the heart (tasdiq), as proven by linguistic opposites, conventional language, and Quranic usage that separates it from actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to have iman, but do you truly believe that your salvation rests solely on the affirmation in your heart, not on your deeds? If your actions were stripped away, would your iman still stand firm before Allah? Today, confront whether you have secretly relied on your good works to feel secure.
?
The opposite of iman is denial (takdhib). Have you ever, even for a moment, denied Allah's commands in your daily choices—like ignoring a prohibition or doubting a promise? The proof from opposition forces you to see that any act of denial chips away at your iman. What will you do now to align your heart's affirmation with your life?
?
If iman is only tasdiq, then your relationship with Allah is defined by your inner conviction, not your outer performance. Does that truth liberate you or unsettle you? Are you willing to examine your heart without the mask of religious actions, and ask: is my tasdiq alive and certain, or merely a habit?
